Key Takeaway
The division of nazar into valid/invalid and clear/unclear is metaphorical, based on the varying clarity of formal and material components of reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Is there an area of your understanding where you have limited a principle like ‘clarity’ to only one type of knowledge (e.g., only proofs) while neglecting definitions? How might expanding your view—seeing that definitions too can range from clear to unclear—change how you learn or teach foundational concepts?
?

Are you currently settling for what is merely clear and obvious, avoiding the harder theoretical work that leads to deeper certainty? What specific ‘theoretical step’ in your own learning or faith have you been postponing? Commit to taking that step this week.

Conditions for Correct Definition and Proof in Nazar
Al-Taftazani states conditions for correctness of form in definition and proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the conditions that make a definition or proof correct in terms of form and matter according to Al-Taftazani.

Core Idea
A correct definition presents the general restricted by a differentia; a correct proof meets syllogistic conditions.

Before classifying nazar, Al-Taftazani outlines what makes a definition or proof valid. For a definition, its form must present a mental image parallel to the definiendum. For a proof, it must follow the rules of syllogism, induction, and analogy. This lesson explores these foundational criteria.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Notice the two parts: one about definition, one about proof.
2
Identify the condition for definition
The passage states that correctness of form in a definition requires starting with the more general (a‘am) and then restricting it by a differentia (fasl) or a special property (khassa). The goal is to form a mental image that matches or distinguishes the definiendum.
This is the logical process of definition by genus and differentia.
3
Identify the condition for proof
For a proof (dalil), it must meet the conditions that produce knowledge, as detailed in the logical chapters on syllogism (qiyas), induction (istiqra'), and analogy (tamthil).
The passage refers to the broader logical framework for correct inference.

Definition of Correct Nazar and the Dispute over Certainty
al-Taftazani defines sound nazar and presents the dispute about yielding certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of sound nazar and the nature of the dispute over whether it yields certainty, including the positions of the Samaniyya, some philosophers, and the Imam's response.

Core Idea
Sound nazar yields certainty, but deniers limit it to opinion only.

In this passage, al-Taftazani explains what makes nazar 'sound' and then dives into a long-standing debate: can systematic reasoning (nazar) produce certainty (yaqin) or only opinion (zann)? He clarifies that everyone agrees it produces opinion; the dispute is about certainty. Various groups deny it, but the Imam's approach is to assert that nazar yielding knowledge does exist in general.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: definition, then dispute, then deniers, then Imam's response.
The passage is concise; pay attention to the key terms.
2
Understand the definition of correct nazar
Identify what makes nazar 'sound' according to the author: it must be accompanied by its conditions and yields knowledge (ilm).
Conditions (shara'it) are not detailed here but emphasized.
3
Identify the point of dispute
Note that there is no dispute about nazar yielding opinion (zann). The real dispute is about whether it yields certainty (yaqin).
This is a key clarification to avoid misunderstanding.
4
Learn the positions of the deniers
The Samaniyya deny certainty absolutely; a group of philosophers deny it in metaphysics and physics; Aristotle reportedly denied it in metaphysics.
Aristotle's quote shows even a great philosopher doubted certainty in this field.
5
See the Imam's response
The Imam (likely al-Razi) does not give a detailed proof but simply affirms that nazar yielding knowledge exists in general, enough to refute the deniers.
The response is minimal but sufficient for the purpose.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النظر الصحيح. قال : والصحيح المقرون بشرائطه يفيد العلم . قال الإمام : لا نزاع في أن النظر يفيد الظن، وإنما النزاع في إفادته اليقين، فأنكره السمنية مطلقاً وجمع من الفلاسفة في الإلهيات والطبيعيات. حتى نقل عن أرسطو أنه قال : لا يمكن تحصيل اليقين في المباحث الإلهية إنما الغاية القصوى فيها الأخذ بالأولى والأخلق، وهذا أقرب بأن يكون محل النزاع، إذ لا يتصور تردد في أن الحاصل من ضرب الاثنين في الاثنين أربع، وبالجملة لما كان مقصود الإمام الرد على المنكرين اقتصر على أن النظر المفيد للعلم مطلقاً أو في الإلهيات موجود في الجملة
The correct nazar. He said: And the correct (nazar) accompanied by its conditions yields knowledge (al-'ilm). Imam said: There is no dispute that nazar yields opinion (al-zann); rather the dispute is about its yielding certainty (al-yaqin). The Samaniyya denied it absolutely, and a group of philosophers in metaphysics (ilahiyyat) and physics (tabi'iyyat). It is even reported from Aristotle that he said: Certainty cannot be obtained in metaphysical inquiries; the ultimate goal therein is to take the most fitting and appropriate. This is closer to being the point of dispute, for no hesitation is conceivable that the result of multiplying two by two is four. In summary, since the aim of the Imam was to refute the deniers, he confined himself to the fact that nazar that yields knowledge, whether absolutely or in metaphysics, exists in general.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage defines correct nazar (al-nazar al-sahih) as that which is accompanied by its conditions (shara'it) and yields knowledge ('ilm). It then presents the dispute about whether such nazar can yield certainty (yaqin). The author notes that all agree that nazar yields opinion (zann); the contestation is about certainty. The Samaniyya (a Hindu sect) denied it entirely, as did some philosophers in metaphysics and physics, with a reported statement from Aristotle that certainty is not attainable in metaphysical matters, only the most appropriate. The Imam (likely Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) responded by simply asserting that nazar yielding knowledge does exist, in a general sense, without needing to prove it for every particular case.

نظر صحيح (nazar sahih)
شرائط (shara'it)
علم ('ilm)
ظن (zann)
يقين (yaqin)
سمنية (Samaniyya)
إلهيات (ilahiyyat)
طبيعيات (tabi'iyyat)
الإمام (al-Imam)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The dispute is about whether nazar yields any knowledge at all.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the dispute is specifically about certainty, not opinion; everyone agrees that nazar yields opinion.

Checkpoint
What is the point of dispute regarding nazar according to the passage?
A) Whether nazar is useful in acquiring knowledge.
B) Whether nazar yields certainty (yaqin) or only opinion (zann).
C) Whether nazar in metaphysics can yield any knowledge.
D) Whether nazar requires conditions to be effective.

Key Takeaway
Sound nazar, when conditions are met, can yield certainty, but this was contested by the Samaniyya and some philosophers, though the Imam affirmed its possibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In which areas of your own faith do you tend to settle for mere opinion (zann) rather than seeking certainty (yaqin) through proper reasoning? What conditions of sound nazar—like sincerity, humility, and following evidence—are you neglecting?
?

?
Aristotle said that in divine matters one can only take 'the most fitting and appropriate.' Is there a part of your heart that secretly agrees—that you will never be certain about the unseen? How does al-Taftazani's insistence that sound nazar yields yaqin confront your hidden doubt? What step will you take today to move from 'most fitting' to certainty?

Al-Amidi's Conditions for Nazar to Yield Knowledge
Enumeration of conditions and a methodological note.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Amidi's two conditions for nazar to produce knowledge and why the restriction to certainties can be replaced by the term 'sahih'.

Core Idea
Nazar must be in certainties and free from obstacles to yield knowledge.

Al-Amidi sought to establish a universal principle for correct nazar. He identified two conditions: the premises must be certain, and no opposite of perception should interrupt the reasoning. This passage explains those conditions and why the restriction to certainties can be subsumed under the term 'sahih'.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how al-Amidi sets up the need for a universal rule and then imposes conditions.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two conditions
Find the two conditions al-Amidi imposes on nazar: (1) being in certainties (qat'iyyat), (2) not being followed by opposites of perception. Note the examples given for the second condition.
The first condition is explicitly stated with 'faqqada al-nazar bi-kawnihi fi al-qat'iyyat'.
3
Understand the omission of the first condition
The author says they omitted the restriction to certainties by simply saying 'sahih' (correct). Understand why: nazar in opinionative matters is materially invalid for seeking knowledge, so it's implicitly excluded.
This shows an economy in terminology.
4
Note the scope including tasawwur
The phrase 'wa li-yatanawwal al-nazar al-matlab bihi al-tasawwur' indicates that the condition about opposites ensures that nazar includes the case of seeking a tasawwur.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما قصد الآمدي إثبات قاعدة تنطبق على الأنظار الجزئية الصحيحة الصادرة عنا في اكتساب العلوم افتقر إلى إثبات الموجبة الكلية فقيد النظر بكونه في القطعيات، إذ النظر في الظني لا يفيد العلم وفاقاً، وبأن لا يعقبه شيء من أضداد الإدراك كالنوم والغفلة والموت، فإنه لا علم حينئذ بل لاظن أيضاً، وجعل كلا الأمور المذكورة ضداً للإدراك على ما هو رأي المتكلمين، وإن من لم يوافق اصطلاح الفلاسفة، وتركنا التقييد بالقطعي استغناء عنـه بـذكـر الصحيح، إذ النظر في الظني لطلب العلم يكون فاسداً من جهة المادة، حيث لم يناسب المطلوب، وليتناول النظر المطلوب به التصور، هذا وظاهر كلام المتكلمين أنهم يريدون بالعلم والنظر عند الإطلاق ما يخص التصديقات، وأن ما ذكرنا في قولهم العلم صفة توجب تمييزاً لا يحتمل النقيض والنظر فكر يطلب به علم أو ظن أنه يعم التصور والتصديق تكلف منا
And when al-Amidi intended to establish a principle that applies to the particular correct nazars issuing from us in acquiring sciences, he needed to establish a universal affirmative, so he restricted nazar to being in certainties (qat'iyyat), because nazar in the opinionative (zanni) does not yield knowledge by agreement, and also that it is not followed by any of the opposites of perception (addad al-idrak) such as sleep, heedlessness, and death, for there is then no knowledge, nor even opinion. And he made each of these mentioned matters an opposite of perception according to the view of the mutakallimun, even if one does not agree with the terminology of the philosophers. And we left the restriction to the certain, dispensing with it by mentioning the correct (sahih), because nazar in the opinionative in seeking knowledge is invalid from the perspective of matter, since it does not suit the sought, and so that it includes nazar by which tasawwur is sought. And this is the apparent meaning of the mutakallimun: that they intend by 'ilm and nazar when unqualified specifically tasdiqat, and that what we mentioned in their statement 'ilm is an attribute that necessitates a distinction that does not accept contradiction (naqid) and nazar is thought by which 'ilm or zann is sought' applies to both tasawwur and tasdiq, but that is a labor from us.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage explains al-Amidi's conditions for nazar to produce knowledge. To have a universal rule for correct particular nazars, al-Amidi requires: (1) that the premises be certain (qat'iyyat), because nazar in opinionative matters does not yield knowledge by consensus; (2) that no opposite of perception (like sleep, heedlessness, death) follow the nazar, otherwise knowledge is absent. These are termed 'opposites of perception' by the mutakallimun. The author notes that they themselves omitted the first restriction by simply saying 'sahih' because nazar in opinionative matters for seeking knowledge is materially invalid. Finally, a terminological point: the mutakallimun, when using 'ilm and nazar without qualification, mean tasdiq (assent), though the definitions they give may include both tasawwur and tasdiq, but that is a stretch.

Key Terms
قطعيات
ظني
أضداد الإدراك
الصحيح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Amidi's conditions are meant to apply to all nazar universally, including invalid ones.
Correction: Al-Amidi is specifically establishing a rule for correct particular nazars (anẓār juzʾiyya ṣaḥīḥa), not for any nazar.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is one of al-Amidi's conditions for nazar to yield knowledge?
A) The nazar must be based on opinion (zann).
B) The nazar must be in certainties (qat'iyyat).
C) The nazar must be followed by sleep or heedlessness.
D) The nazar must be conducted by a philosopher.

Key Takeaway
Al-Amidi's conditions require nazar to be in certainties and free from perceptual obstacles, but the first condition can be subsumed under 'sahih' to include tasawwur.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about matters of your faith, do you rely on certainties from revelation and reason, or do you settle for weak opinions? This lesson tells you that nazar in opinionative matters does not yield knowledge. Are you building your belief on sand? What will you do today to seek certainty in at least one area of your deen?
?
The passage warns that heedlessness (ghafla) is an opposite of perception that prevents nazar from yielding knowledge. Is your search for truth often interrupted by spiritual heedlessness? How can you actively guard your heart and mind to stay present in your reflection? Commit to one concrete habit this week.
?
The author notes that by using 'sahih', the condition of certainty is subsumed and includes tasawwur (conceptual understanding). The Mutakallimun's Usage of 'Ilm and Nazar
Clarification of terminological usage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the mutakallimun use the terms 'ilm and nazar when unqualified, and why the general definitions seem to include both tasawwur and tasdiq.

Core Idea
For the mutakallimun, 'ilm and nazar unqualified refer to tasdiq, not tasawwur.

The mutakallimun have a specific terminology: when they use the words 'ilm and nazar without qualification, they mean tasdiq (assent). However, the definitions they give—such as 'ilm as an attribute necessitating distinction that does not admit contradiction—appear to cover both tasawwur and tasdiq. This passage clarifies that applying those definitions to both is a forced interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Focus on the contrast between what the mutakallimun intend and what the definitions imply.
The phrase 'ẓāhir kalām al-mutakallimīn' indicates the apparent meaning.
2
Identify the intended meaning
The mutakallimun unqualified use of 'ilm and nazar is specific to tasdiqat (assents). This is the default understanding.
3
Understand the tension with definitions
The definitions given earlier (e.g., 'ilm as an attribute that necessitates distinction not admitting contradiction') seem to apply to both tasawwur and tasdiq. The author says that extending them to both is a 'takalluf' (labor or forced interpretation).
This highlights a nuance in terminology.
4
Reflect on the significance
This distinction matters for understanding kalam arguments: when a mutakallim says 'ilm must be about tasdiq, not tasawwur, it affects what counts as certain knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما قصد الآمدي إثبات قاعدة تنطبق على الأنظار الجزئية الصحيحة الصادرة عنا في اكتساب العلوم افتقر إلى إثبات الموجبة الكلية فقيد النظر بكونه في القطعيات، إذ النظر في الظني لا يفيد العلم وفاقاً، وبأن لا يعقبه شيء من أضداد الإدراك كالنوم والغفلة والموت، فإنه لا علم حينئذ بل لاظن أيضاً، وجعل كلا الأمور المذكورة ضداً للإدراك على ما هو رأي المتكلمين، وإن من لم يوافق اصطلاح الفلاسفة، وتركنا التقييد بالقطعي استغناء عنـه بـذكـر الصحيح، إذ النظر في الظني لطلب العلم يكون فاسداً من جهة المادة، حيث لم يناسب المطلوب، وليتناول النظر المطلوب به التصور، هذا وظاهر كلام المتكلمين أنهم يريدون بالعلم والنظر عند الإطلاق ما يخص التصديقات، وأن ما ذكرنا في قولهم العلم صفة توجب تمييزاً لا يحتمل النقيض والنظر فكر يطلب به علم أو ظن أنه يعم التصور والتصديق تكلف منا
And when al-Amidi intended to establish a principle that applies to the particular correct nazars issuing from us in acquiring sciences, he needed to establish a universal affirmative, so he restricted nazar to being in certainties (qat'iyyat), because nazar in the opinionative (zanni) does not yield knowledge by agreement, and also that it is not followed by any of the opposites of perception (addad al-idrak) such as sleep, heedlessness, and death, for there is then no knowledge, nor even opinion. And he made each of these mentioned matters an opposite of perception according to the view of the mutakallimun, even if one does not agree with the terminology of the philosophers. And we left the restriction to the certain, dispensing with it by mentioning the correct (sahih), because nazar in the opinionative in seeking knowledge is invalid from the perspective of matter, since it does not suit the sought, and so that it includes nazar by which tasawwur is sought. And this is the apparent meaning of the mutakallimun: that they intend by 'ilm and nazar when unqualified specifically tasdiqat, and that what we mentioned in their statement 'ilm is an attribute that necessitates a distinction that does not accept contradiction (naqid) and nazar is thought by which 'ilm or zann is sought' applies to both tasawwur and tasdiq, but that is a labor from us.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage explains al-Amidi's conditions for nazar to produce knowledge. To have a universal rule for correct particular nazars, al-Amidi requires: (1) that the premises be certain (qat'iyyat), because nazar in opinionative matters does not yield knowledge by consensus; (2) that no opposite of perception (like sleep, heedlessness, death) follow the nazar, otherwise knowledge is absent. These are termed 'opposites of perception' by the mutakallimun. The author notes that they themselves omitted the first restriction by simply saying 'sahih' because nazar in opinionative matters for seeking knowledge is materially invalid. Finally, a terminological point: the mutakallimun, when using 'ilm and nazar without qualification, mean tasdiq (assent), though the definitions they give may include both tasawwur and tasdiq, but that is a stretch.

Correction: In the mutakallimun's technical usage, when unqualified, 'ilm and nazar are specific to tasdiqat, not tasawwurat.

Key Takeaway
In mutakallimun terminology, 'ilm and nazar unqualified mean tasdiq, although definitions may seem broader.

The Philosophers' View on Nazar and Emanation
Philosophers' theory of nazar preparing mind for emanation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' theory that nazar prepares the mind to receive knowledge emanating from the active intellect, rather than directly producing it.

Core Idea
Nazar prepares the mind, not to create knowledge, but to receive it from the active intellect.

This lesson introduces a key philosophical concept that contrasts with earlier discussed views of the Ash'aris and Mu'tazila. According to the philosophers, human reasoning (nazar) does not directly produce knowledge but rather prepares the mind to receive an overflow (fayadan) of knowledge from an external source—the active intellect. Understanding this theory is essential for grasping the full spectrum of epistemological positions in classical Islamic thought.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the key terms: 'nazar', 'prepares the mind', 'overflow of knowledge', 'Giver of Forms', 'active intellect'.
The passage is dense; take your time to absorb each phrase.
2
Identify the components of the theory
List the agent (active intellect), the recipient (mind/soul), the action (preparation), and the result (emanation of knowledge). Notice the causal chain: nazar → mind prepared → knowledge overflows from active intellect.
3
Contrast with other views
Compare this view with the Ash'ari position (nazar as direct cause of knowledge through God's custom) and the Mu'tazila view (nazar generates knowledge through tawlid). How does the philosophical view differ in its reliance on an external source?
This comparison helps solidify the distinctiveness of each school.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider what this theory implies about human agency and the source of knowledge. If knowledge comes from an external active intellect, what role remains for human effort?
Think about how this aligns with the philosophers' overall emanationist cosmology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعند الفلاسفة هي النظر يعد الذهن لفيضان العلم عليه من عند واهب الصور، الذي هو عندهم العقل الفعال المنتقش بصور الكائنات المفيض على أنفسنا
And according to the philosophers, it (nazar) prepares the mind for the overflow (fayadan) of knowledge upon it from the Giver of Forms (Wahib al-Suwar), which according to them is the active intellect (al-'aql al-fa''al) imprinted with the forms of all beings, causing overflow upon our souls.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage briefly describes the philosophical view that nazar (reasoned reflection) prepares the mind (yudhi adh-dhahn) for the overflow (fayadan) of knowledge upon it from the Giver of Forms (Wahib as-Suwar), which they call the active intellect (al-'aql al-fa''al). This active intellect is said to be imprinted (mumtashshiq) with the forms of all creatures and causes emanations upon our souls. This is a distinct epistemological theory contrasting with the Ash'ari and Mu'tazila views discussed earlier.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: Nazar directly produces knowledge through human effort alone.
Correction: According to the philosophers, nazar only prepares the mind; the actual knowledge comes as an emanation from the active intellect (the Giver of Forms).

Checkpoint

B) It prepares the mind to receive emanation from the active intellect.
C) It generates knowledge through tawlid.
D) It is irrelevant to acquiring knowledge.

Key Takeaway
In the philosophical view, nazar prepares the mind not to create knowledge but to receive it through emanation from the active intellect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

If all patterns of knowledge are already present in a transcendent source, what does that imply about the importance of seeking knowledge? Are you actively seeking to align your mind with that source, or are you content with superficial understanding?

The philosophers' view on reflection and the active intellect
The author presents the philosophers' doctrine that reflection prepares the mind for emanation from the active intellect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' doctrine that reflection prepares the mind for emanation from the Active Intellect, and their identification of the Active Intellect with the Preserved Tablet and Clear Book.

Core Idea
Reflection does not create knowledge; it readies the soul to receive from the Active Intellect.

This passage presents the philosophers' (falasifa) perspective on how reflection (nazar) leads to knowledge. According to them, reflection is not a direct cause but a preparation for receiving emanation from the Active Intellect. Understanding this doctrine is essential for contrasting it with the Ash'ari and Maturidi views on knowledge acquisition.

Nazar Necessarily Yields Knowledge in All Syllogistic Forms
Al-Taftazani argues for the necessity of nazar in all syllogistic forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Taftazani argues that speculative reasoning (nazar) necessarily produces knowledge in all syllogistic forms, not just the first, given the proper conditions.

Core Idea
Speculative reasoning yields necessary knowledge in every syllogistic form when the aspect of indication is observed.

Al-Taftazani engages with a key debate in Islamic logic: does speculative reasoning (nazar) produce knowledge necessarily in all syllogistic forms, or only in the first? Some scholars held that necessity is limited to the first figure, while others like al-Razi and Imam al-Haramayn took more extreme positions. In this passage, Taftazani defends the view that, under the right conditions, nazar is necessarily productive in every form, though the clarity may vary. He introduces the crucial role of 'observing the aspect of indication' (jiha al-dalala) as a condition that makes all figures equally clear.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation to grasp the overall argument. Notice the technical terms like 'الشكل الأول' and 'جهة الدلالة'.
The passage begins with a dense conditional statement about dependence of knowledge.
2
Identify the main thesis and the objection
Pinpoint al-Taftazani's thesis: nazar is necessarily useful for knowledge in all forms. Then find the objection: some say it's necessary only in the first figure. Locate the phrase 'فإن قيل' (if it is said) to spot the objection.
The objection is based on apparent obviousness of necessity in the first figure.
3
Analyze the response and conditions
Examine the response beginning with 'قلنا'. Taftazani distinguishes between the general rule (nazar itself) and particularities. He then introduces the condition of observing the aspect of indication (jiha al-dalala) and subsumption (indiraj), which levels the figures.
The example of the mule illustrates heedlessness without this condition.
4
Reflect on the argument's significance
Consider why Taftazani insists on the necessity in all figures. This underscores that sound reasoning depends not just on form but on the reasoner's awareness of how premises indicate the conclusion. It connects to broader discussions in Islamic epistemology.
The passage ends by referring to a fuller treatment in another work.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن ضروري العلم بالنتيجة لما توقف على العلم بالكبرى الكلية التي من جملة أفراد موضوعها . موضوع النتيجة لزم توقف النتيجة على نفسها وكونها قبل أن تعلم وهو تناقض وذلك لأن معلومية الحكم كحدوث العالم من جهة كون المحكوم عليه من أفراد الأوسط كالمتغير لا يناقض مجهوليته من جهة كونه من أفراد الأصغر، أعني العالم فإن قيل لا خفاء في أن كون النظر مفيداً للعلم ضروري في الشكل الأول، نظري في باقي الأشكال. فكيف يصح اختيار أنه مطلقاً على ما ذهب إليه الرازي أو نظري مطلقاً على ما ذهب إليه إمام الحرمين ...؟ قلنا : الكلام فيما إذا أخذ عنوان الموضوع هو النظر، فيقال : النظر أو كل نظر على شرائطه يفيد العلم، وما ذكر من التفصيل قطعاً إنما هو في الخصوصيات مثل قولنا العالم متغير، مع قولنا وكل متغير حادث أو ولا شيء من القديم بمتغير فإن العلم بإفادة الأول ضروري والثاني نظري على أن هذا التفصيل إنما هو بالنظر إلى مجرد ترتيب المقدمتين ووضع الحد الأوسط عن الحدين الآخرين، وأما بعد حصول جميع الشرائط فالحكم بإفادة كل من خصوصيات النظر العلم ضروري في جميع الأشكال على ما يراه بعض المحققين من أن من جملة الشرائط ملاحظة جهة دلالة المقدمتين على المطلوب، وكيفية اندراجه فيهما بالقوة حتى قال الإمام حجة الإسلام إن هذا هو السبب الخاص لحصول النتيجة بالفعل وبدونه ربما يذهل عن النتيجة مع حضور المقدمتين، كما إذا رأى بغلة منتفخة البطن فتوهم أنها حامل مع ملاحظة أنها بغلة عاقر وكل بغلة ولا خفاء في أن مع ملاحظة جهة الإنتاج والتفطن لكيفية الاندراج يتساوى الأشكال في الجلاء حتى ذهب بعض أهل التحقيق إلى أن الكل حينئذ يرجع إلى الشكل الأول بحسب التعقل وإن لم يتمكن من تلخيص العبارة فيه، وتمام تحقيقه في شرح الأصول لصاحب المواقف
That knowledge of the conclusion is necessary when it depends on knowledge of the universal major premise from whose individuals the subject of the conclusion is part, which would necessitate the conclusion's dependence on itself and its being before it is known, which is a contradiction. That is because the knownness of the judgment—like the world's temporal origination from the aspect that the subject is an individual of the middle term, such as 'changing'—does not contradict its unknownness from the aspect that it is an individual of the minor term, i.e., the world. If it is said: There is no hiddenness in that the fact that speculative reasoning is useful for knowledge is necessary in the first figure and theoretical in the remaining figures. So how can one validly choose that it is absolutely [necessary] according to al-Razi, or absolutely theoretical according to Imam al-Haramayn? — We say: The discussion is when the topic (s) is taken as 'speculative reasoning,' so it is said: Speculative reasoning, or every speculative reasoning under its conditions, yields knowledge. What was mentioned of the distinction is definitely only about particularities, such as our saying 'The world is changing' together with our saying 'Every changing thing is temporally originated' or 'Nothing eternal is changing.' For knowledge of the first's benefit is necessary and the second theoretical. However, this distinction is only with respect to the mere arrangement of the two premises and placing the middle term relative to the other two terms. But after all conditions are fulfilled, the judgment that each particular speculative reasoning yields knowledge is necessary in all figures, according to what some verifiers hold: that among the conditions is observing the aspect of the two premises' indication of the sought conclusion, and the manner of its subsumption in them virtually, such that the Imam Hujjat al-Islam said this is the specific cause for obtaining the result potentially, and without it one might be heedless of the result despite the presence of the two premises, as when one sees a mule with a swollen belly and imagines it is pregnant while noting that it is a barren mule, and all mules [are barren]. There is no hiddenness that with observance of the aspect of production and understanding the manner of subsumption, the figures become equal in clarity, such that some verifiers held that everything then reverts to the first figure in terms of intellection, even if one cannot summarize the expression in it. And its full verification is in the commentary on al-Usul by the author of al-Mawaqif.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage defends the thesis that speculative reasoning (nazar) necessarily yields knowledge in all syllogistic forms, contrary to the view that it is necessary only in the first figure and theoretical in others. The author begins by pointing out a contradiction in the opposing view: if knowledge of the conclusion depends on the universal major premise, then the conclusion itself would be required for its own knowledge. He clarifies that the knownness of a judgment from one perspective does not contradict its unknownness from another. Then an objection is raised: it is obvious that nazar is necessary in the first figure but theoretical in others. The author responds that when nazar is taken as a subject, it is always productive under proper conditions. The distinction between necessary and theoretical applies only to particular premises, not to the general rule. Furthermore, after all conditions are met—including observing the aspect of indication (jiha al-dalala) and the subsumption (indiraj)—the judgment is necessary in all figures. He cites al-Ghazali's example of the mule: someone seeing a mule with a swollen belly might mistakenly think it is pregnant because they fail to subsume the minor premise under the major. With attention to the aspect of production, all syllogistic forms become equally clear, even reverting to the first figure conceptually. The passage ends by referring to a more complete verification in another work.

Key Terms
النظر
العلم
الضروري
النظري
الشكل الأول
جهة الدلالة
الاندراج
الإمام حجة الإسلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the necessity of nazar for knowledge depends solely on the syllogistic form (e.g., first figure only).
Correction: The passage argues that necessity depends on proper conditions, especially observing the aspect of indication, which makes all forms equally productive.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is a necessary condition for nazar to produce knowledge in any syllogistic form?
A) The syllogism must be in the first figure.
B) The premises must be universally quantified.
C) Observing the aspect of the two premises' indication of the sought conclusion (jiha al-dalala).
D) The conclusion must be known prior to the premises.

Key Takeaway
Al-Taftazani establishes that speculative reasoning, when the reasoner actively observes how premises indicate the conclusion, necessarily produces knowledge in all syllogistic forms, countering the view that necessity is limited to the first figure.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you engage in reasoning about matters of faith, do you actively seek to understand how your premises indicate the conclusion, or do you rely solely on the form of your argument? Recall a time your reasoning fell short—was it because you neglected the 'aspect of indication'? What will you change in your approach to seeking knowledge?
?
Al-Ghazali's example of the mule shows that even with correct premises, we can err if we are heedless. In what area of your religious understanding might you be like the person who sees the mule's belly and jumps to a conclusion? What premise have you overlooked, and how can you rectify that heedlessness starting today?
?
This lesson argues that under the right conditions, reasoning yields necessary knowledge. Have you settled for conjecture in matters where certainty is possible? What conditions—such as careful observation of indications—are you missing? Commit to one practical step to deepen your reasoning in a specific area of your faith.

Objection and Rebuttal on the Subsumption Condition
Al-Taftazani presents an objection and rebuttal on the subsumption condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that the subsumption condition leads to an infinite regress of premises is considered weak and how al-Taftazani rebuts it.

Core Idea
The subsumption condition does not add a new premise; it merely observes how premises relate.

In this passage, al-Taftazani addresses a potential objection raised by Imam al-Razi against the necessity of being aware of subsumption (al-indiraj) for a syllogism to produce knowledge. The objection claims that if subsumption is a distinct condition, it would lead to an infinite regress of required premises. The author argues that this objection misunderstands the nature of subsumption.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to get the overall argument.
The passage contains an objection and a rebuttal.
2
Identify the objector's claim
Find the part where al-Razi argues that subsumption being a separate known entity would create an infinite regress of premises.
The phrase 'يفضي إلى اعتبار ما لا نهاية لها من المقدمات' hints at this.
3
Understand the author's rebuttal
Notice how al-Taftazani responds: the subsumption is not a separate proposition but an observation ('ملاحظة') of the relationship between premises.
The word 'ملاحظة' is key.
4
Reflect on the logical distinction
Consider why distinguishing between a premise and an observation matters for the syllogism's structure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم كلام القوم هو أن العلم يكون التفطن للاندراج شرطاً للإنتاج ضروري، وحديث البغلة تنبيه عليه، ومنع الإمام على ما قال ان ذلك إنما يكون عند حصول إحدى المقدمتين فقط. وأما عند اجتماعهما فلا نسلم إن كان الشك في النتيجة مكابرة، واستدلاله على بطلان ذلك بأن الاندراج لو كان معلوماً مغايراً للمقدمتين لكانت مقدمة أخرى مشروطة في الإنتاج فينقل الكلام إلى كيفية التئامها مع الأوليين، ويفضي إلى اعتبار ما لا نهاية لها من المقدمات ضعيف، لأن ذلك ملاحظة لكيفية نسبة المقدمتين إلى النتيجة لاقضية هي جزء القياس ليكون مقدمة، على أنه لو سمي مقدمة أو جعل عبارة عن التصديق يكون الأصغر بعض جزئيات الأوسط التي حكم على جميعها بالأكبر، فليس بلازم أن يكون له مع المقدمتين هيئة واندراج شرط العلم بها لتحصل مقدمة رابعة وهلم جراً.
Then the speech of the people is that knowledge of being attentive to the subsumption is a necessary condition for production, and the story of the mule is a reminder of it. The Imam [al-Razi] denied that, saying that that only occurs when one of the two premises is obtained. But when both are together, we do not concede that if there is doubt about the conclusion, it is stubbornness. His argument for the invalidity of that—that if the subsumption were known and different from the two premises, it would be another premise conditioned for production, which would transfer the discussion to how it coheres with the first two and lead to considering an infinite number of premises—is weak, because that is an observation of how the two premises relate to the conclusion, not a proposition that is part of the syllogism to be a premise. Even if it were called a premise or made an expression of belief, the minor term is some particular of the middle term about all of which the major term is judged, so it is not necessary that it have, along with the two premises, a form and subsumption whose knowledge is a condition for a fourth premise to result, and so on.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage presents an objection-and-answer pair. The objector, identified as 'the Imam' (likely al-Razi), challenges the view that awareness of the subsumption (al-indiraj) is a necessary condition for a syllogism to produce knowledge. He argues that if the subsumption were known and distinct from the two premises, it would constitute a third premise, leading to an infinite regress of premises. The author rebuts this, calling the objection weak. He explains that the subsumption is not a separate proposition or premise; rather, it is an observation of how the two premises relate to the conclusion. Even if one were to call it a premise, it would not generate an infinite regress because the minor term is already a particular of the middle term, and the subsumption is simply the recognition of that relation, not an additional distinct premise that would require further premises.

Key Terms
الاندراج
المقدمة
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The subsumption condition adds a formal premise to the syllogism, causing an infinite regress of premises.
Correction: The subsumption is not a separate premise but an observation of the relation between the premises; thus no regress occurs.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the objection that the subsumption condition leads to an infinite regress of premises?
A) Because the subsumption is identical to one of the premises.
B) Because the subsumption is not a separate proposition but an observation of the relation between premises and conclusion.
C) Because an infinite regress is accepted in logic.
D) Because the Imam's argument is based on a false example.

Key Takeaway
The subsumption condition does not introduce an infinite regress because it is not a separate premise but an observation of the relationship between premises.

Answering the Regress Objection Against Reflection
Sanusi answers the first objection that knowledge from reflection must be either necessary or regressive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the claim that knowledge from reflection leads to an infinite regress.

Core Idea
Theoretical knowledge is self-validating; it does not require another reflection.

Sanusi now tackles the first major objection to using reflection (nazar) as a source of knowledge. The opponent argues that if knowledge from reflection is self-evident (necessary) then it is trivial, but if it is theoretical, it would need further reflection to be confirmed, leading to an endless chain. Sanusi's response is swift and direct: the knowledge itself is self-sufficient, like knowing that there is no counterargument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic once, then the translation. Notice the structure: the objection is stated concisely, then the answer begins with 'قلنا'.
The passage is a single sentence with two clauses: objection and answer.
2
Analyze the objection's two branches
Identify the two possibilities: if knowledge from reflection is necessary (self-evident) then its truth is obvious; if it is theoretical then it would require another reflection, causing regress.
The opponent thinks both options are problematic.
3
Understand the answer's denial
Sanusi denies that after correct reflection the knowledge becomes evident, and he also denies that knowing it is knowledge depends on another reflection. Instead, the knowledge itself is self-sufficient.
The key phrase: 'بل يحصل به نفسه' – it obtains by itself.
4
Reflect on the analogy
The author gives an analogy: like knowing that there is no counterargument. This knowledge is immediate and doesn't need proof.
This shows how self-validation works in practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : العلم يكون ما يحصل عقيب النظر علماً إن كان ضرورياً لم يظهر خلافه وإن كان نظرياً تسلسل، قلنا: ظهور بعد النظر الصحيح ممنوع ، وكذا توقف العلم بأنه علم على نظر آخر ، بل يحصل به نفسه كالعلم بأنه لا معارض
First: Knowledge is what is obtained after reflection. If it is necessary, its status as knowledge is evident; if it is theoretical, it leads to an infinite regress. We say: Its becoming evident after correct reflection is denied, and similarly the knowledge that it is knowledge depending on another reflection [is denied]. Rather, it obtains by itself, like knowing that there is no counterargument.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage records the first objection of an opponent and the author's response. The opponent argues that if knowledge comes from reflection (nazar), then either it is necessary (self-evident) or theoretical. If necessary, its validity is immediately apparent; if theoretical, it requires another reflection to confirm it, leading to an infinite regress. The author responds by denying that knowledge becomes evident after correct reflection, and also denies that knowing it is knowledge depends on a separate reflection. Instead, the knowledge itself is self-sufficient, analogous to knowing that no counterargument exists—this knowledge does not need further proof.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Theoretical knowledge always requires a separate act of reflection to verify its validity.
Correction: Sanusi argues that theoretical knowledge is self-validating; it obtains by itself, like knowing there is no counterargument.

Checkpoint

B) Through intuition
C) The knowledge itself is self-sufficient; no further reflection is needed
D) Through divine revelation

Key Takeaway
Theoretical knowledge from correct reflection is self-validating and does not require an infinite regress of verification.

Answering the Objection: Proof and Ignorance
Sanusi answers the objection that proof contradicts ignorance condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection regarding the condition of ignorance and how Sanusi resolves it by clarifying that knowledge follows the proof.

Core Idea
Knowledge follows the proof; ignorance is not a contradictory condition.

The objector argues that if a proof yields knowledge, then requiring ignorance beforehand is contradictory. Sanusi's brief but crucial response distinguishes between ignorance as a prerequisite and knowledge as a consequence. This lesson unpacks that distinction and its methodological significance.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the compact style: the objection is stated, then denied, then clarified.
The key term 'يستقعبه' (follows) is the heart of the answer.
2
Identify the two parts
The passage has two clear parts: the objection (إفادته العلم تنافي اشتراط عدم العلم) and the response (قلنا ممنوع ...). Understand what the opponent claims and how Sanusi counters.
3
Understand the resolution
Sanusi denies the contradiction by reinterpreting the condition: ignorance is not a positive requirement but simply the absence of knowledge before the proof. The proof then necessarily generates knowledge.
This preserves both the condition (that one does not already know) and the effect of the proof (that it produces knowledge).
4
Apply the principle
Consider why this distinction matters for methodology: proofs in kalam are meant to produce certainty, not to presuppose it. The condition of ignorance is about the starting point, not a permanent state.
This objection touches on the very nature of proof and its relationship to knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : إفادته العلم تنافي اشتراط عدم العلم قلنا ممنوع ، فإن المراد أنه يستقعبه
Second: Its giving knowledge contradicts the condition of not having knowledge. We say: Denied, for what is meant is that it [knowledge] follows it [the proof].
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection and the brief response. The opponent claims that if a proof gives knowledge, it would be incompatible with the condition that the person must not already have that knowledge (i.e., the condition of ignorance). The author denies this, explaining that the intended meaning is that knowledge follows the proof, not that ignorance is a positive condition. The proof itself produces knowledge, so there is no contradiction.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) By clarifying that knowledge follows the proof, not that ignorance is required
D) By accepting the objection

Key Takeaway
The proof gives knowledge, and that knowledge follows from the proof, so there is no contradiction with the condition that one does not know beforehand.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Third Objection: If Proof Gives Knowledge, Seeking Would Be Absurd
Sanusi answers the objection that obtaining knowledge through effort would be pointless if it could be attained.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection against the utility of proofs and the response that the obligation is to seek knowledge, which is within human capacity.

Core Idea
Effort in seeking knowledge is a duty, not a pointless burden.

Sanusi addresses a common objection: if a proof can deliver certain knowledge, why would anyone be required to strive for it? The response clarifies that the obligation is to seek, and that seeking is possible. This lesson explains this exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the concise structure: the opponent's claim followed by the author's rebuttal.
2
Understand the opponent's reasoning
The opponent argues that if proof yields certain knowledge, then God commanding people to burden themselves to obtain it would be morally repugnant (qabih). Why would this be considered ugly?
The underlying assumption is that if something is attainable easily, demanding strenuous effort is unfair.
3
Grasp the author's response
Sanusi replies that the obligation (taklif) is to acquire knowledge (tahsiluhu), and that acquisition is within one's capacity (mashud). This means the duty is to strive, not to guarantee attainment.
4
Reflect on the principle
Consider how this response defends the role of effort in religious knowledge. It establishes that seeking knowledge is a commanded act, not a pointless exercise.
This connects to broader themes of human responsibility and divine wisdom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : لو أفاده البتة لقبح التكلف بالعلم . قلنا : التكليف بتحصيله وهو مقدور
Third: If it [the proof] gave certain knowledge, then burdening oneself with seeking knowledge would be considered ugly. We say: The obligation is to acquire it, and it is within one's capacity.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection and response. The opponent argues that if a proof could yield certain knowledge, then it would be unreasonable (ugly) for God to obligate humans to seek that knowledge, because they could simply be given it. The author replies that the obligation is to seek knowledge, and that seeking is within human capability. Thus there is no absurdity; the obligation is tied to action, not to the guarantee of attainment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If knowledge is attainable by proof, then there is no need for effort.
Correction: The obligation is to seek knowledge, not to receive it without effort. The duty is to strive within one's capacity.

Checkpoint

C) He agrees that it is absurd
D) He says the proof is not certain

Key Takeaway
The obligation to seek knowledge remains valid even if knowledge is attainable through proof; the duty is to strive within one's capacity.

Fourth objection: disagreement on the self proves difficulty, not impossibility
Sanusi answers the objection that the soul is a near subject yet disputed, so more distant subjects are even harder.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the objection that disagreement about the human self proves that distant metaphysical subjects are unknowable.

Core Idea
Disagreement signals difficulty, not impossibility.

A common sceptical argument is that if the most intimate object of knowledge—the self—is a subject of endless dispute, then surely more remote matters are even more doubtful. Sanusi counters by distinguishing between impossibility and difficulty. In this passage, you will see how he uses this distinction to defend the possibility of theological knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: the opponent first makes a claim, then Sanusi responds.
The objection is stated concisely; the response is equally brief.
2
Identify the opponent's reasoning
The opponent argues that the self is closest to us, yet disagreement about it is immense. Therefore, more distant subjects must be even harder to know, implying impossibility.
3
Analyse Sanusi's response
Sanusi makes a key distinction: disagreement indicates difficulty (ʿusr), not impossibility (imtinaʿ). This preserves the possibility of knowledge while acknowledging the effort required.
The phrase 'لا يدل على الامتناع بل على العسر' is the core of the response.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this distinction is important for kalam. If disagreement meant impossibility, much of theology would be invalid. By showing it only means difficulty, Sanusi keeps the door open for rational inquiry.
Difficulty implies the need for rigorous investigation, not abandonment of the subject.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : أقرب الأشياء إلى الإنسان هويته، وقد كثر فيها الخلاف كثرة لا تنضبط، فكيف فيما هو أبعد ؟ قلنا : لا يدل على الامتناع بل على العسر ولا نزاع فيه
Fourth: The closest thing to a person is his self, yet disagreement about it has increased beyond measure, so how about what is more distant? We say: It does not indicate impossibility, but rather difficulty, and there is no dispute about that.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth objection and response. The opponent argues that the human self is the nearest object of knowledge, yet there is extensive disagreement about it, so how can one claim to know things that are more distant (e.g., metaphysical matters)? The author responds that the disagreement indicates difficulty (usur), not impossibility (imtinaʿ). Thus attaining knowledge about difficult subjects is hard but not impossible, and no one denies the difficulty.

Key Terms
هوية
خلاف
عسر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many assume that widespread disagreement on a topic proves that the topic is unknowable or impossible to ascertain.
Correction: Disagreement only shows that the topic is difficult, not impossible. Difficulty does not entail impossibility.

Checkpoint
What does the author conclude from the disagreement about the human self?
A) That knowledge about oneself is impossible
B) That knowledge about distant matters is impossible
C) That disagreement shows difficulty, not impossibility
D) That the objection is valid

Key Takeaway
Disagreement about a subject signals its difficulty, not its impossibility; therefore, the existence of controversy does not invalidate the pursuit of knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Entailment vs. Knowledge of Entailment
The author distinguishes between entailment and knowledge of entailment in nazar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between objective logical entailment and subjective knowledge of that entailment, and why this distinction is crucial in evaluating the products of nazar.

Core Idea
Entailment is objective; knowing it depends on the knower's preparedness.

In this passage, the author addresses a subtle but critical point in the theory of reasoning (nazar). He defends the necessity of the condition of 'being mindful of how the minor falls under the major' by clarifying that logical entailment itself is distinct from the psychological awareness of that entailment. This distinction explains why different minds may reach the conclusion at different speeds, even if the logical relationship is the same.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the key claim
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice that the author begins by stating a conditional scenario about equality of moods, then gives a rebuttal.
The rebuttal starts with 'for we say' (لأنا نقول).
2
Understand the distinction
Focus on the sentence 'فرق بين لزوم الشيء والعلم بلزومه'. This is the heart of the lesson. The author separates the logical fact from the mental awareness.
3
Apply the distinction
Consider how this distinction resolves the objection that all moods should produce equal certainty. The entailment is equal, but awareness conditions vary.
This shows why the condition of 'being mindful' is needed despite logical entailment being universal.
4
Reflect on the implications
Think about how this principle applies to your own learning: the truth is one, but our access to it depends on our preparation.
This is a foundational point in Islamic logic and epistemology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يفيد القطع بهذا الاشتراط، لأن القياس المقرون بالشرائط ملزوم للنتيجة انفکاکه قطعاً، واللازم يمتنع عن الملزوم ، فلو لم يكن التفطن لكيفية الاندراج شرطاً متفاوت الحصول بأن يحصل في البعض بمجرد الالتفات وفي البعض بوسط خفي أو أخفى ، لزوم استواء جميع الضروب المنتجة في حصول النتيجة عند حصولها ضرورة امتناع انفكاك اللازم عن الملزوم المستجمع لشرائط اللزوم، لأنا نقول فرق بين لزوم الشيء والعلم بلزومه فالضروب والأشكال متساوية في لزوم النتائج إياها ، بمعنى حقيتها في نفس الأمر على تقدير حقيتها، وإنما التفاوت في العلم بذلك ، وشروطه متفاوتة الحصول كالالتفات أو الاكتساب بخفي أو أخفى ، وإن لم يكن التفطن لكيفية الاندراج من جملتها .
It yields certainty with this condition, because the syllogism coupled with its conditions entails the conclusion necessarily, the necessary cannot be separated from the condition, so if being mindful of how the minor falls under the major were not a condition of varying attainment—that it is attained in some by mere attention and in others by a subtle or subtler middle term—then all productive moods would be equal in obtaining the result when they occur, by virtue of the impossibility of the separation of the necessary from the condition that possesses all conditions of entailment. For we say: there is a difference between the entailment of a thing and knowledge of its entailment. The moods and figures are equal in the entailment of results by them, meaning their truth in themselves assuming their truth, but the variation is in the knowledge of that, and its conditions are of varying attainment, such as attention or acquisition by subtle or subtler means, even if being mindful of how the minor falls under the major is not among them.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage defines the key distinction between objective entailment (luzum) and subjective knowledge of that entailment. The author argues that while a valid syllogism (qiyas) necessarily yields its conclusion, the condition of 'being mindful of how the minor falls under the major' (al-tafattun li-kayfiyyat al-indiraj) varies in difficulty. If this condition were not required, all moods would produce knowledge equally, but actually the variation is in the knower's awareness, not in the logical entailment itself. Thus the author rejects the objection that all moods should produce equal certainty by pointing out that entailment and knowledge of entailment are distinct.

Key Terms
التفطن لكيفية الاندراج
اللزوم
العلم بلزومه
الضروب المنتجة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Logical entailment always produces immediate knowledge.
Correction: Entailment is objective and independent of the knower, but knowledge of that entailment depends on the knower's mental conditions, such as attention and intermediate concepts.

Checkpoint
What is the main distinction the author makes in this passage?
A) The difference between necessary and theoretical knowledge.
B) The difference between entailment of a thing and knowledge of that entailment.
C) The difference between a valid and an invalid syllogism.
D) The difference between attention and acquisition.

Key Takeaway
There is a crucial difference between a conclusion being entailed by premises and a person knowing that entailment; the latter depends on mental conditions that vary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you learn a new Islamic ruling, do you often assume that understanding the proof means you have truly grasped its entailment? In what area of your life has this mistaken assumption led to overconfidence or neglect of deeper reflection?
?
The author says that knowing entailment requires conditions like attention and mental effort. Think of a specific aqeedah topic you previously accepted without deeply understanding. Are you prepared to reinvestigate it with the proper mental care, even if it challenges your comfort?
?
You have heard the argument for the existence of God many times, but do you genuinely know the entailment of the proof's premises, or have you merely memorized the conclusion? What would it take for you to move from superficial acceptance to actual certainty?

First Three Objections to Nazar and Their Responses
First three objections and responses on nazar and knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first three objections raised against nazar producing certain knowledge and how the author refutes them.

Core Idea
Nazar yields knowledge conditionally, not automatically.

The opponents of kalam raise six objections to show that nazar cannot produce certain knowledge. The first three objections concern the nature of knowledge obtained, the role of prior knowledge, and the rationality of obligation. In this lesson, we examine the author's crisp refutations.

Guided Path
1
Read the objections and responses
Read the first three objections and the author's responses (qulna) carefully. Notice the concise style.
Each objection is followed by a rebuttal.
2
Analyze the first objection
The first objection claims infinite regress if knowledge is theoretical. The response denies the appearance of contrary after correct nazar and denies the need for a second nazar.
The author uses the example of knowing no conflicting evidence.
3
Understand the second and third objections
The second objection says nazar giving knowledge contradicts the condition of absence of prior knowledge; the response clarifies nazar 'follows' knowledge. The third says obligation would be absurd if nazar already gives knowledge; the response says obligation is to acquire it, which is possible.
These rebuttals defend nazar as a valid method.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قال) : احتج المخالف بوجوه الأول : العلم يكون ما يحصل عقيب النظر علماً إن كان ضرورياً لم يظهر خلافه وإن كان نظرياً تسلسل، قلنا: ظهور بعد النظر الصحيح ممنوع ، وكذا توقف العلم بأنه علم على نظر آخر ، بل يحصل به نفسه كالعلم بأنه لا معارض الثاني : إفادته العلم تنافي اشتراط عدم العلم قلنا ممنوع ، فإن المراد أنه يستقعبه الثالث : لو أفاده البتة لقبح التكلف بالعلم . قلنا : التكليف بتحصيله وهو مقدور - والرابع : أقرب الأشياء إلى الإنسان هويته، وقد كثر فيها الخلاف كثرة لا تنضبط، فكيف فيما هو أبعد ؟ قلنا : لا يدل على الامتناع بل على العسر ولا نزاع فيه الخامس : شرط التصديق وسو [؟] التصور منتف في الحقائق الإلهية - قلنا ممنوع . السادس : لو صح الاستدلال على الصانع بدليل فموجبه: إما ثبوت الصانع فيلزم انتفاؤه على تقدير انتفائه ، وإما العلم به فلا يكون دليلا عند عدم النظر فيه قلنا لا نعني بإفادته ودلالته إلا كونه بحيث متى وجد وجد المدلول ومتى نظر فيه علم المدلول فلا يلزم من انتفائه انتفاؤه، ولا من عدم النظر فيه انتفاء الحيثية .
(He said): The opponent argued with several points: First: If the knowledge that results from nazar is necessary, then its contrary would not appear; if it is theoretical, then there is infinite regress. We say: That it appears after correct nazar is denied. Similarly, that knowledge of it being knowledge depends on another nazar is denied; rather, knowledge itself is obtained by it, like knowledge that there is no conflicting evidence. Second: That nazar gives knowledge contradicts the condition of absence of prior knowledge. We say: Denied; what is meant is that it follows it. Third: If it gave knowledge absolutely, then being burdened with knowledge would be absurd. We say: The obligation is to acquire it, and it is within capacity. Fourth: The closest thing to man is his self, yet there is great disagreement about it, so how about what is more remote? We say: This does not indicate impossibility but difficulty, and there is no dispute about that. Fifth: The condition of assent and concept is absent in divine matters. We say: Denied. Sixth: If inference to the Maker were valid by a proof, then its implication would be either the existence of the Maker, in which case its non-existence would follow from its non-existence, or knowledge of Him, in which case it would not be a proof when not considered. We say: We mean by its benefit and indication only that it is such that whenever it exists the indicated exists, and whenever one considers it one knows the indicated; so its non-existence does not imply His non-existence, nor does non-consideration imply absence of the aspect.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage presents six numbered objections to the idea that nazar (theoretical reflection) yields certain knowledge ('ilm). Each objection is followed by a response (qulna). The first objection argues that if the knowledge from nazar is necessary, its contrary would not appear; if theoretical, it leads to infinite regress. The response denies the appearance of contrary after correct nazar and denies the need for a second nazar to know that knowledge—knowledge itself is immediate. The second objection claims that nazar giving knowledge contradicts the condition of not having prior knowledge; the response clarifies that nazar is meant to 'follow' the knowledge, not precede it. The third objection says if nazar already gave knowledge, being commanded to seek it would be absurd; the response explains the obligation is to acquire it, which is possible. The fourth objection notes that even the self is disputed, so more remote matters are harder; the response admits difficulty but denies impossibility. The fifth objection states that the conditions of tasdiq and tasawwur are absent in divine matters; the response flatly denies this. The sixth objection presents a dilemma about the proof for the Maker leading to circularity; the response defines the proof's function as conditional existence and consideration, not as implying the Maker's non-existence if not considered or if the proof is absent.

Key Terms
النظر
العلم
الضروري
النظري
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Nazar should produce knowledge instantly or else it's invalid.
Correction: The author shows that nazar produces knowledge conditionally: after correct reflection, and it does not require a subsequent reflection to confirm it.

Checkpoint
According to the response to the first objection, what does the author deny?
A) That correct nazar can produce knowledge
B) That the contrary appears after correct nazar and that knowledge of it being knowledge depends on another nazar
C) That nazar is a valid method
D) That knowledge can be necessary

Key Takeaway
The first three objections are refuted by clarifying the nature of nazar: it is a conditional, non-redundant, and capacity-based process.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever avoided deep thinking about your beliefs because you assumed you already knew enough? How might that be an excuse for neglecting nazar?
?
When you hear an objection to your faith, do you immediately dismiss it or engage with it? The lesson shows that objections can be answered—what objection are you avoiding right now?
?
The third response says acquiring knowledge is within your capacity. Where in your life have you convinced yourself that seeking truth is too hard? Will you commit to one step of nazar today?

Objections Four to Six on Nazar and the Proof for the Maker
Three objections on difficulty, concept, and inference

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the remaining three objections against nazar and the author's responses, especially the sixth objection about the proof for the Maker.

Core Idea
Difficulty does not equal impossibility; inference conditional.

The final three objections shift from the nature of knowledge to the difficulty of investigating divine matters and the logic of inference. The fourth objection appeals to disagreement even about the self; the fifth denies the possibility of conceptualization; the sixth presents a dilemma about the proof for the Creator. The author's responses are decisive and instructive.

Guided Path
1
Read the objections and responses
Read the fourth, fifth and sixth arguments and the responses. Note the pattern of objection and rebuttal.
2
Examine the fourth objection
The fourth objection says even the self is disputed, so more remote matters are harder. The response says this indicates difficulty, not impossibility.
The author concedes difficulty but denies the conclusion of impossibility.
3
Understand the fifth and sixth
The fifth objection claims the conditions of tasdiq and tasawwur are absent in divine matters; the response flatly denies. The sixth objection presents a dilemma about inference to the Maker; the response redefines the proof's benefit as conditional existence and consideration.
The sixth objection is the most sophisticated.

General Objection and Rebuttal on Nazar's Efficacy
A general objection and rebuttal, with clarification on the dispute's scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the general objection against all six responses on nazar's efficacy and the author's rebuttal, including why a broader doubt was excluded.

Core Idea
The objection that 'nazar does not yield knowledge' would itself be self-refuting or self-evident.

This passage presents a sweeping objection that challenges all six previous responses at once. The author refutes it by showing that the knowledge used in the objection would either be theoretical (leading to circularity) or necessary (making the dispute trivial). He then explains why an even broader doubt—that nazar produces no belief at all—was not included earlier: the actual dispute is about certainty, not opinion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Let the author's argument flow naturally before analyzing it.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the objection
Notice the objection: 'the knowledge that nazar does not yield knowledge'—if theoretical, it's self-refuting; if necessary, it would be obvious and not disputed.
This is a classic dilemma.
3
Understand the rebuttal to the retort
The author counters a possible defense ('opposing corruption with corruption') by showing that if that defense leads to absurdity, the desired conclusion stands; otherwise it is invalid.
This is a meta-argument.
4
Grasp why the previous doubt was excluded
The author clarifies that the earlier doubt negates that nazar produces any belief (tasdiq) absolutely, but the dispute is only about complete certainty (yaqin), not about opinion (zann).
This narrows the scope of disagreement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأورد على الكل أن العلم بأن النظر لا يفيد العلم إن كان نظرياً استفيد منها فيناقض، وإن كان ضرورياً نبه عليه لم يقع فيه خلاف أكثر العقلاء . فإن قيل : عارضنا الفاسد بالفاسد قلنا : إن أفادت الفساد ثبت المطلوب وإلا لغت . وإنما لم نورد الشبهة السابقة في ضمن الوجوه لأنها لنفي أن يكون التصديق الحاصل عقيب النظر علماً مطلقاً أو في الطبيعيات والإلهيات : أو في الإلهيات خاصة على ما ذكره الإمام من أنه لا نزاع في إفادة النظر الظن وإنما النزاع في إفادته اليقين الكامل، وينبغي ألا تكون العدديات محل الخلاف والشبهة السابقة تنفي كون النظر مفيداً للتصديق مطلقاً .
And it was objected against all [the previous responses] that the knowledge that nazar does not yield knowledge—if this knowledge is theoretical, it is derived from it, leading to contradiction; if it is necessary, it [i.e., the contrary] would not have occurred, and most rational people would not have disputed it. If it is said: 'We are opposing corruption with corruption,' we say: If it yields corruption, the desired [conclusion] is established; otherwise it is null. And we only did not include the previous doubt among the points because it is for negating that the belief resulting from nazar is knowledge absolutely, or in natural and divine matters, or in divine matters specifically, as the Imam mentioned that there is no dispute over nazar producing opinion (zann), but only over whether it produces complete certainty (yaqin), and arithmetical matters should not be the locus of dispute, and the previous doubt negates that nazar produces belief (tasdiq) absolutely.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage presents a general objection against all six responses: the knowledge that 'nazar does not yield knowledge' would itself be either theoretical (leading to self-refutation) or necessary (which would be obvious and not disputed). The response counters a possible retort that 'we are opposing corruption with corruption' by stating that if that opposing argument yields corruption (i.e., absurdity), the desired conclusion stands; otherwise it is invalid. The author then explains why he did not include this doubt among the previous six: it is aimed at negating that the belief from nazar is knowledge absolutely, or in specific domains, whereas the Imam (al-Razi?) holds that the dispute is only about certain knowledge (yaqin), not about opinion (zann).

Key Terms

العلم
الظن
اليقين
التصديق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The earlier doubt applies to all types of knowledge, including opinion, and should have been included among the six objections.
Correction: The author clarifies that the earlier doubt negates nazar producing belief (tasdiq) absolutely, whereas the actual dispute is only about whether nazar produces complete certainty, not about producing opinion. Hence, it was excluded.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why was the 'previous doubt' not included among the six objections?
A) Because it was invalid and not worth mentioning.
B) Because it negates that nazar produces any belief at all, while the dispute is about certainty.
C) Because it applies only to arithmetical matters.
D) Because it was already answered in the sixth objection.

Key Takeaway
The general objection fails because it either leads to self-refutation or is self-evident; the author then clarifies that the real dispute is about certainty, not opinion.
