The Refutation: Clarifying the Meaning of Absolute Obligation
Refutation: Clarifying the meaning of absolute obligation and refuting the objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's refutation of the fourth objection, which clarifies that absolute obligation means obligation on condition of the existence of its prerequisites, not obligation in every state.

Core Idea
Absolute obligation only requires the condition of the prerequisite, not universal application.

In response to the objection that knowledge cannot be absolutely obligatory, the author provides a nuanced refutation. He explains that 'absolute obligation' does not mean obligation in every state—otherwise no obligation would be absolute. Instead, it means obligation on the supposition that the prerequisite exists. He illustrates this with examples from fasting and pilgrimage, showing that obligations are often conditional. The lesson also addresses the related issue of the impossibility of attaining what is already attained.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation passage
Read the Arabic and translation of the refutation. Note how the author redefines absolute obligation and provides examples.
The author uses examples from fasting and pilgrimage.
2
Understand the new definition
The author states that absolute obligation means obligation on the condition of the existence of the prerequisite and its non-existence. Grasp how this applies to knowledge.
3
Analyze the examples
Consider the examples of fasting (absolute with respect to intention, restricted by residence) and pilgrimage (restricted by ability). See how this clarifies absolute obligation.
These examples show that obligations are often conditional, yet considered absolute in a technical sense.
4
Reflect on the tahsīl al-ḥāṣil objection
The author notes that the obligation of knowledge is not for the knower, but for the ignorant. The principle of tahsīl al-ḥāṣil does not apply because the obligation is conditional upon absence of knowledge.
5
Note the deferred refutation
The author mentions another problem regarding doubt and absence of knowledge but defers it to a later discussion. Understand why this is left aside.
The dispute concerns whether doubt and ignorance are within one's power and whether they can be obligatory.

The Fifth Objection: Revisiting the Obligation of Prerequisites
Author refutes the fifth objection that prerequisites of absolute obligations are not obligatory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate about whether prerequisites of absolutely obligatory acts are themselves obligatory, and the author's defense that commanding a non-volitional act like knowledge entails commanding its cause.

Core Idea
Commanding an effect can only mean commanding its cause when the effect is not directly voluntary.

In this passage, the author tackles a subtle objection: if God commands something absolutely (like knowledge), does He automatically command its prerequisite (like reflection)? The objector says no—commands can be issued without commanding prerequisites. The author refines the argument by distinguishing between the existence of a thing and the obligation of its prerequisite, and ultimately shows that for non-volitional acts, commanding the cause is the only coherent way to command the effect.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the dialectical rhythm of objection and response come through.
Notice the back-and-forth structure: 'If it is said... we say...'
2
Identify the two rounds of objection
The objector raises two arguments: first, that obligating something without its prerequisite is impossible; second, that if the prerequisite can be omitted, the original obligation becomes impossible. Identify where each appears.
3
Follow the author's distinctions
The author distinguishes between the impossibility of the commanded act's existence without the prerequisite and the obligation of the prerequisite itself. He further distinguishes legal permissibility of abandonment from explicit command.
These distinctions are key to the refutation.
4
Understand the analogy and the conclusion
The author uses the analogy of commanding killing to show that commanding an act implicitly commands its means. For knowledge, which is not a voluntary act but a quality, the only way to command it is to command its cause (nazar).
This resolves the objection without relying on the permissibility of obliging the impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : أنا لا نسلم أن مقدمة الواجب المطلق يلزم أن تكون واجبة لجواز إيجاب الشيء مع الذهول عن مقدمته، بل مع التصريح بعدم وجوبها . فإن قيل : إيجاب الشيء بدون مقدمته تكليف بالمحال ضرورة استحالة الشيء بدون ما يتوقف عليه قلنا المستحيل وجود الشيء بدون وجود المقدمة، ولا تکلیف به، وإنما التكليف بوجود الشيء بدون وجوب المقدمة، ولا استحالة فيه . فإن قيل : - لو لم تجب مقدمة الواجب المطلق لجاز تركها شرعاً، مع بقاء التكليف بالأصل لكونه واجباً مطلقاً، أي على تقدير وجود المقدمة وعدمها، ولا خفاء في أنه مع عدم المقدمة محال فيكون التكليف به حينئذ تكليفاً بالمحال . قلنا : عدم جواز ترك الشيء شرعاً قد يكون لكونه لازماً للواجب الشرعي فيكون واجباً أنه لا بد منه وهذا لا يقتضي كونه مأموراً به متعلقاً بخطاب الشارع على ما هو المتنازع والجواب تخصيص الدعوى، وهو المأمور به إذا كان شيئاً ليس في وسع العبد إلا مباشرة أسباب حصوله، كان إيجابه إيجاباً لمباشرة السبب قطعاً كالأمر بالقتل، فإنه أمر باستعمال الآلة، وحز الرقبة مثلاً، وههنا العلم نفسه ليس فعلاً مقدوراً بل كيفية، فلا معنى لإيجابه إلا إيجاب سببه، الذي هو النظر ، وليس هذا مبنياً على امتناع تكليف المحال حتى يرد الاعتراض بأنه جائز عندكم
The fifth: We do not concede that the prerequisite of an absolutely obligatory act must necessarily be obligatory, because it is permissible to make a thing obligatory while being heedless of its prerequisite, indeed even while explicitly stating that it is not obligatory. If it is said: 'Making something obligatory without its prerequisite is an obligation of the impossible, necessarily because of the impossibility of a thing without that on which it depends,' we say: What is impossible is the existence of the thing without the existence of the prerequisite, and there is no obligation to that. Rather, the obligation is to the existence of the thing without the obligation of the prerequisite, and there is no impossibility in that. If it is said: 'If the prerequisite of an absolutely obligatory act were not obligatory, it would be legally permissible to abandon it while the obligation of the original act remains, because it is absolutely obligatory – i.e., on the supposition of the existence of the prerequisite and its non-existence. And it is obvious that without the prerequisite it is impossible, so the obligation at that time would be an obligation of the impossible,' we say: The legal impermissibility of abandoning something may be because it is necessary for a legal obligation, and thus it is obligatory and indispensable. But this does not entail that it is commanded, related to the address of the Lawgiver, as is the point of dispute. And the answer is a specification of the claim: The commanded act, when it is something not within the servant's power except by directly undertaking its causes, its obligation is an obligation of directly undertaking the cause, definitely – like the command to kill, for it commands the use of the tool and cutting the neck, for example. And here, knowledge itself is not a volitional act but a quality, so there is no meaning to its obligation except the obligation of its cause, which is nazar. And this is not based on the impossibility of obliging the impossible so as to meet the objection that it is permissible according to you.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the fifth objection: that the prerequisite (muqaddama) of an absolutely obligatory act need not itself be obligatory. The objection uses the fact that God might command something without commanding its prerequisite or even while stating the prerequisite is not obligatory. The author responds by distinguishing between the impossibility of the commanded act without the prerequisite and the obligation of the prerequisite. He then addresses a further argument that if the prerequisite can be omitted, the original obligation becomes impossible, leading to an obligation of the impossible (taklīf bi’l-muhāl). The answer is that the legal impermissibility of abandoning the prerequisite may arise from its being necessary for the obligation, but that does not mean it is commanded by a separate address. The key insight is that when the commanded thing (knowledge) is not a voluntary act but a quality (kayfiyya), the only way to command it is to command its cause (nazar). This is analogous to commanding killing, which implicitly commands the use of tools. The author notes that this solution does not rely on the Ash‘arī position on the permissibility of obliging the impossible.

تكليف بالمحال

كيفية

Correction: The author shows that for non-volitional acts like knowledge, the prerequisite (nazar) is necessarily commanded as the only means to the act, even if not separately addressed.

Checkpoint

Sufficiency of Consensus and Scripture for Obligation of Nazar
Author concludes that consensus and Quranic verses alone prove the obligation of nazar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the obligation of nazar is firmly established by consensus and the apparent meanings of Quranic verses, making elaborate metaphysical premises unnecessary.

Core Idea
Consensus and Quranic proofs alone suffice to establish the obligation of nazar.

In this concluding passage, the author steps back from the intricate kalam arguments and reminds us of a simpler foundation. He affirms that the obligation of rational inquiry (nazar) is not dependent on complex philosophical premises—it is rooted in the consensus of scholars and the clear words of the Quran. This lesson will help you appreciate the decisive role of scriptural evidence in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation

3

4
Understand the author's purpose
Why does the author make this point here? He is showing that the previous complex arguments are not strictly necessary.
5
Reflect on the significance
This shows that Islamic theology is grounded in revelation, not just reason alone.

واعلم أنه لما كان المقصود وجوب النظر شرعياً، وقد وقع الإجماع عليه كما صرحوا به فلا حاجة إلى ما ذكروا من المقدمات، ودفع الاعتراضات بل لو قصد إثبات مجرد الوجوب دون أن يكون بدليل قطعي لكفى التمسك بظواهر النصوص كقوله تعالى : فانظر إلى آثار رحمة الله وقل انظروا ماذا في السموات والأرض إلى غير ذلك.

Commentary
This passage serves as a concluding remark. The author states that the real goal is to establish that nazar (rational inquiry) is legally obligatory (wajib shar‘ī). Since there is consensus (ijmā‘) on this, the lengthy dialectical premises and refutations of objections are unnecessary. Moreover, even without a definitive proof, the clear apparent meanings (zawāhir) of Quranic verses—such as 'Look at the effects of God's mercy' (Sūrat al-Rūm 30:50) and 'Say: Look at what is in the heavens and the earth' (Sūrat Yūnus 10:101)—are sufficient to prove the obligation. This shows that the elaborate arguments earlier were not strictly necessary; the consensus and the scriptural texts provide a solid foundation.

The Fifth Objection: The Premise of an Absolute Obligation Need Not Be Obligatory
The author presents the fifth objection and its response about the obligation of a premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the premise of an absolute obligation is not necessarily obligatory and how the author defends this position against two key objections.

Core Idea
An absolute obligation does not automatically make its premises obligatory.

In this passage, the author tackles a persistent objection in uṣūl al-fiqh and kalām: if something is absolutely obligatory, does that mean its necessary premise is also obligatory? The objector claims yes, otherwise the original would be impossible. The author offers a nuanced response, distinguishing between the existence of a premise and its legal obligation, and showing that only when the commanded act is not within one's direct power does the obligation extend to its cause. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the full translation. Notice the flow of objection and response.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two objections
Find the first objection: obligating without premise is impossible. Then find the second objection: if premise not obligatory, one could abandon it, making original impossible.
The author replies to each in turn.
3
Analyze the author's key distinctions
In the first reply, note the distinction between existence of a premise and its obligation. In the second, note the difference between a necessary concomitant (lāzim) and a commanded obligation.
These distinctions are central to the argument.
4
Understand the specification
The author specifies that when the commanded act is not within one's capacity except via its causes, then the obligation extends to the cause—like knowledge requiring reflection.
This shows why reflection is obligatory.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The author notes that consensus on the obligation of reflection suffices; elaborate proofs are unnecessary, and one could rely on Quranic verses.
This closes the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : أنا لا نسلم أن مقدمة الواجب المطلق يلزم أن تكون واجبة لجواز إيجاب الشيء مع الذهول عن مقدمته، بل مع التصريح بعدم وجوبها . فإن قيل : إيجاب الشيء بدون مقدمته تكليف بالمحال ضرورة استحالة الشيء بدون ما يتوقف عليه قلنا المستحيل وجود الشيء بدون وجود المقدمة، ولا تكليف به، وإنما التكليف بوجود الشيء بدون وجوب المقدمة، ولا استحالة فيه . فإن قيل : لو لم تجب مقدمة الواجب المطلق لجاز تركها شرعاً، مع بقاء التكليف بالأصل لكونه واجباً مطلقاً، أي على تقدير وجود المقدمة وعدمها، ولا خفاء في أنه مع عدم المقدمة محال فيكون التكليف به حينئذ تكليفاً بالمحال . قلنا : عدم جواز ترك الشيء شرعاً قد يكون لكونه لازماً للواجب الشرعي فيكون واجباً أنه لا بد منه وهذا لا يقتضي كونه مأموراً به متعلقاً بخطاب الشارع على ما هو المتنازع والجواب تخصيص الدعوى، وهو المأمور به إذا كان شيئاً ليس في وسع العبد إلا مباشرة أسباب حصوله، كان إيجابه إيجاباً لمباشرة السبب قطعاً كالأمر بالقتل، فإنه أمر باستعمال الآلة، وحز الرقبة مثلاً، وههنا العلم نفسه ليس فعلاً مقدوراً بل كيفية، فلا معنى لإيجابه إلا إيجاب سببه، الذي هو النظر، وليس هذا مبنياً على امتناع تكليف المحال حتى يرد الاعتراض بأنه جائز عندكم، واعلم أنه لما كان المقصود وجوب النظر شرعياً، وقد وقع الإجماع عليه كما صرحوا به فلا حاجة إلى ما ذكروا من المقدمات، ودفع الاعتراضات بل لو قصد إثبات مجرد الوجوب دون أن يكون بدليل قطعي لكفى التمسك بظواهر النصوص كقوله تعالى : فانظر إلى آثار رحمة الله وقل انظروا ماذا في السموات والأرض إلى غير ذلك.
Fifth: We do not concede that the premise of an absolute obligation is necessarily obligatory, because it is permissible for a thing to be obligatory while one is heedless of its premise, even with explicit statement that it is not obligatory. If it is said: Making a thing obligatory without its premise is an obligation of the impossible, for it is impossible for a thing to exist without that on which it depends. We say: What is impossible is the existence of a thing without the existence of the premise, and there is no obligation for that; rather the obligation is for the existence of the thing without the obligation of the premise, and there is no impossibility in that. If it is said: If the premise of an absolute obligation were not obligatory, it would be permissible to leave it legally, while the obligation of the original remains because it is absolute obligatory, i.e., on both the assumption of the premise's existence and non-existence. It is clear that with the non-existence of the premise it [the original] is impossible, so then the obligation for it would be an obligation of the impossible. We say: The legal impermissibility of leaving something may be because it is a necessary concomitant of a legal obligation, so it becomes obligatory in the sense that it is indispensable; but this does not entail that it is commanded by the Legislator's address, which is the point of dispute. The answer is a specification of the claim: if the commanded thing is something not within the servant's capacity except by directly undertaking its causes, then making it obligatory is necessarily making the cause obligatory, like the command to kill: it is a command to use the tool and cut the neck, for example. Here, knowledge itself is not an action within capability but a quality, so there is no meaning to making it obligatory except making its cause obligatory, which is reasoning. This does not depend on the impossibility of obligating the impossible such that the objection (that it is permissible according to you) would be raised. Know that since the intended point is the legal obligation of reasoning, and consensus has occurred on it as they stated, there is no need for the premises and refutations they mentioned. Rather, if one intended to establish mere obligation without it being by a definitive proof, it would suffice to adhere to the apparent meanings of the texts, like His saying: 'Look then at the effects of Allah's mercy' (30:50) and 'Say: Look at what is in the heavens and the earth' (10:101) and so forth.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth objection and its response regarding the obligation of the premise of an absolute obligation (muqaddimat al-wajib al-mutlaq). The author begins by stating his position: we do not concede that the premise of an absolute obligation must itself be obligatory, because it is possible for something to be obligatory while one is unaware of its premise, or even when one explicitly states the premise is not obligatory. The objector then argues: making something obligatory without its premise is obligating the impossible, since the thing cannot exist without the premise. The author replies that the impossibility is only the existence of the thing without the existence of the premise, and there is no obligation for that; rather the obligation is for the existence of the thing without the obligation of the premise, which is not impossible. Another objection follows: if the premise were not obligatory, it would be legally permissible to abandon it, yet the original obligation remains regardless of the premise's existence; but without the premise the original is impossible, leading to an obligation of the impossible. The author responds: legal impermissibility of abandoning something may arise because it is a necessary concomitant (lazim) of the legal obligation, making it obligatory in the sense of being indispensable. But this does not mean it is commanded by the Legislator's address, which is the point of dispute. The true answer is a specification of the claim: when the commanded thing is something not within the servant's power except by directly undertaking its causes, then making it obligatory is necessarily making the cause obligatory, like the command to kill, which implies using the tool. Here, knowledge (al-'ilm) is not an action within one's power but a quality; so making it obligatory only means making its cause, which is reasoning (al-nazar), obligatory. This does not depend on the impossibility of obligating the impossible. The author concludes that the intended point is the legal obligation of reasoning, and since consensus has occurred on it, there is no need for these elaborate premises and refutations. One could simply rely on the apparent meanings of Quranic texts.

تكليف بالمحال
وجوب النظر
مباشرة السبب

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The premise of an absolute obligation must be obligatory under all circumstances.
Correction: The author argues that the premise need not be obligatory; only when the commanded act is not within one's capacity does obligation extend to its cause, and even then it is not a direct command but a logical consequence.

Checkpoint

D) It is only obligatory if explicitly stated.

Key Takeaway
The premise of an absolute obligation is not necessarily obligatory; only when the commanded act is not within one's capacity does obligation extend to its cause, and for knowledge, that cause is reflection (al-nazar).

The First Obligation: End or Means?
Al-Taftazani presents the disagreement and the correct distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the first wajib depends on whether we mean the intended end (knowledge of Allah) or the means (reflection/intention).

Core Idea
The first obligation is knowledge of Allah as the goal, but reflection as the means.

This lesson presents a classic debate in kalam: what is the very first thing a person must do upon reaching accountability? Some say it is knowledge of Allah, others say it is reflection or the intention to reflect. Al-Taftazani clarifies that the correct answer hinges on what we mean by 'first obligation'.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the disagreement
Read the Arabic and translation. Note that scholars disagreed on what the first wajib is: knowledge, reflection, or doubt.
The passage begins with 'اختلفوا في أول الواجبات'.
2
Understand the correct distinction
The correct view distinguishes between the wajib that is intended in itself (maqsūd bi-dhātihi) and the wajib that is a means to it. If the former, it is knowledge; if the latter, it is reflection or intention.
This distinction resolves the disagreement.
3
Analyze why knowledge is not commanded directly
Knowledge is not within one's power (maqdur), so it cannot be an immediate obligation. Rather, one must work towards it through reflection.
The passage says 'وأما المعرفة فليس بمقدور'.
4
Reflect on the refutation of doubt
The view that doubt is the first wajib is rejected because doubt is a quality, not an act, and is not volitional.
The passage refutes this with 'ورد بأنه ليس بمقدور لكونه من الكيف كالعلم'.
5
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to ensure you grasp the main point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الخامس : اختلفوا في أول الواجبات فقيل : معرفة الله تعالى لأنها الأصل ، وقيل : النظر فيها أو القصد إليه لتوقفها عليه ، والحق أنه إن قيد الواجب بما يكون مقصوداً في نفسه فالأول. وإلا فالثاني. وأما المعرفة فليس بمقدور، إذ الوجوب مقيد به واستدامته ليست بمقدمة . وقيل : الشك لأن النظر بعده ، ورد بأنه ليس بمقدور لكونه من الكيف كالعلم ولا مقدمة لتأتي النظر عند الظن والوهم، وإن أريد به ما يتناولهما وجعل مقدوراً بمعنى إمكان تحصيله فوجوب النظر مقيد به، إذ لا نظر عند الجزم والواجب على المقلد أو الجاهل جهلاً مركباً هو النظر في وجه الدلالة، ليقوده إلى العلم . ما يجب على المكلف، فقال الشيخ : هو معرفة الله تعالى لكونها مبنى الواجبات وقال الأستاذ : هو النظر في معرفة الله تعالى لما مر من كونه المقدمة . وقال القاضي والإمام : هو القصد إلى النظر لتوقف النظر عليه، والحق أنه إن أريد أول الواجبات المقصودة بالذات فهو المعرفة، وإن أريد الأعم فهو القصد إلى النظر، لكن مبناه على وجوب مقدمة الواجب المطلق، وقد عرفت ما فيه، فلذا قال في المتن وإلا فالثاني، أي النظر أو القصد إليه.
The Fifth Section: They differed regarding the first obligations. It was said: knowledge of Allah the Exalted, because it is the foundation. And it was said: reflection (al-nazar) therein, or the intention (al-qasd) to it, because it depends on it. The truth is that if the obligated (al-wajib) is restricted to what is intended in itself, then the first; otherwise, the second. As for knowledge, it is not within one's power (maqdur), for the obligation is conditioned by it, and its continuance is not a prerequisite. And it was said: doubt (al-shakk), because reflection follows it. It was refuted by the fact that it is not within one's power, being a quality (kayf) like knowledge, and it is not a prerequisite so that reflection can occur in the case of conjecture and illusion. And if by it is meant what encompasses them both and it is considered within one's power in the sense of the possibility of acquiring it, then the obligation of reflection is conditioned by it, for there is no reflection when there is certainty. And the obligation on the blind follower (al-muqallid) or the one ignorant with compound ignorance is reflection on the aspect of evidence to lead him to knowledge. What is obligatory on the accountable person (al-mukallaf)? Al-Shaykh said: it is knowledge of Allah the Exalted because it is the basis of obligations. And al-Ustadh said: it is reflection on knowledge of Allah the Exalted for what has been mentioned of it being the prerequisite. And al-Qadi and al-Imam said: it is the intention to reflect, because reflection depends on it. The truth is that if what is meant is the first of the obligations intended in themselves, then it is knowledge; and if what is meant is the more general, then it is the intention to reflect, but its basis is on the obligation of the prerequisite of the absolute obligation (al-wajib al-mutlaq), and you have known what is in it. Therefore, he said in the text: 'otherwise, the second', meaning reflection or the intention to it.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage is a presentation of different opinions on the first obligation (al-wajib) for a person accountable before God. It begins by stating the disagreement: some say it is knowledge of God, because all other obligations are built upon it. Others say it is reflection (nazar) or the intention (qasd) to reflect, because knowledge depends on reflection. The correct view (al-haqq) distinguishes between two meanings of 'first obligation': if by that we mean what is sought for its own sake, then knowledge is first; if we mean the means to it, then reflection or the intention to reflect is first. The passage then explains that knowledge is not directly within one's power (maqdur), so it cannot be commanded as an act; rather, one must work towards it. It then mentions the view that doubt is the first obligation, because reflection follows doubt, but this is refuted on the grounds that doubt is a quality, not an act, and is not volitional. The passage then clarifies the obligation of the blind follower and the one with compound ignorance: they must reflect on the evidence to reach knowledge. It then cites the opinions of specific scholars: al-Shaykh (Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i?) holds knowledge; al-Ustadh (Abu Ishaq al-Isfara'ini?) holds reflection; al-Qadi (al-Baqillani) and al-Imam (al-Razi) hold intention to reflect. The passage concludes by affirming the correct position as per the matn: if the first obligation is understood as the intended end, it is knowledge; otherwise, it is reflection or intention to reflect. The commentary follows the author's order: statement of disagreement, presentation of each view with brief reasoning, the correct distinction, and the specific scholarly opinions.

Key Terms
أول الواجبات
معرفة الله
النظر
القصد إلى النظر
الشك
الواجب المطلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the first obligation is always the same act, e.g., knowledge, regardless of context.
Correction: The passage shows that the first obligation depends on definition: if intended in itself, it is knowledge; if means, it is reflection/intention. Also, doubt is rejected as a candidate.

Checkpoint

A) Reflection (al-nazar)
B) Knowledge (ma'rifa) of Allah
C) Doubt (al-shakk)
D) Intention to reflect (al-qasd ila al-nazar)

Key Takeaway
The first obligation is knowledge of Allah as the ultimate goal, but the first commanded act is reflection or intention to reflect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Obligation of the Muqallid and the Scholars' Views
Al-Taftazani states the obligation of the muqallid and presents the scholars' views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the person in a state of blind faith or compound ignorance must reflect, and that scholars differed on the first obligation.

Core Idea
The muqallid and jahil murakkab must reflect on evidence; scholars agree knowledge is the goal but differ on the means.

After establishing the general principle, al-Taftazani applies it to two categories: the blind follower (muqallid) and the one with compound ignorance (jahil murakkab). He then cites the opinions of four major scholars and reaffirms the correct view from the matn.

Guided Path
1
Read about the muqallid and jahil murakkab
The passage states that for the blind follower or the one with compound ignorance, the first obligation is nazar (reflection) on the evidence to lead them to knowledge.
This shows that the first wajib varies by situation.
2
Identify the four scholars' positions
List each scholar and their view: al-Shaykh (knowledge), al-Ustadh (reflection), al-Qadi and al-Imam (intention to reflect). Note the reasoning given for each.
The passage says 'فقال الشيخ: هو معرفة الله...'
3
Understand the final resolution
The author reaffirms the correct view: if by 'first' we mean the intended end, it is knowledge; if we mean the means, it is intention to reflect. This matches the earlier distinction.
The passage ends with 'فلذا قال في المتن وإلا فالثاني'.
4
Consider the basis of this ruling
The author notes that this whole discussion depends on the obligation of the prerequisite of the absolute obligation (wujūb muqaddimat al-wājib al-muṭlaq), which was discussed earlier.
This shows the interconnectedness of theological concepts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الخامس : اختلفوا في أول الواجبات فقيل : معرفة الله تعالى لأنها الأصل ، وقيل : النظر فيها أو القصد إليه لتوقفها عليه ، والحق أنه إن قيد الواجب بما يكون مقصوداً في نفسه فالأول. وإلا فالثاني. وأما المعرفة فليس بمقدور، إذ الوجوب مقيد به واستدامته ليست بمقدمة . وقيل : الشك لأن النظر بعده ، ورد بأنه ليس بمقدور لكونه من الكيف كالعلم ولا مقدمة لتأتي النظر عند الظن والوهم، وإن أريد به ما يتناولهما وجعل مقدوراً بمعنى إمكان تحصيله فوجوب النظر مقيد به، إذ لا نظر عند الجزم والواجب على المقلد أو الجاهل جهلاً مركباً هو النظر في وجه الدلالة، ليقوده إلى العلم . ما يجب على المكلف، فقال الشيخ : هو معرفة الله تعالى لكونها مبنى الواجبات وقال الأستاذ : هو النظر في معرفة الله تعالى لما مر من كونه المقدمة . وقال القاضي والإمام : هو القصد إلى النظر لتوقف النظر عليه، والحق أنه إن أريد أول الواجبات المقصودة بالذات فهو المعرفة، وإن أريد الأعم فهو القصد إلى النظر، لكن مبناه على وجوب مقدمة الواجب المطلق، وقد عرفت ما فيه، فلذا قال في المتن وإلا فالثاني، أي النظر أو القصد إليه.
The Fifth Section: They differed regarding the first obligations. It was said: knowledge of Allah the Exalted, because it is the foundation. And it was said: reflection (al-nazar) therein, or the intention (al-qasd) to it, because it depends on it. The truth is that if the obligated (al-wajib) is restricted to what is intended in itself, then the first; otherwise, the second. As for knowledge, it is not within one's power (maqdur), for the obligation is conditioned by it, and its continuance is not a prerequisite. And it was said: doubt (al-shakk), because reflection follows it. It was refuted by the fact that it is not within one's power, being a quality (kayf) like knowledge, and it is not a prerequisite so that reflection can occur in the case of conjecture and illusion. And if by it is meant what encompasses them both and it is considered within one's power in the sense of the possibility of acquiring it, then the obligation of reflection is conditioned by it, for there is no reflection when there is certainty. And the obligation on the blind follower (al-muqallid) or the one ignorant with compound ignorance is reflection on the aspect of evidence to lead him to knowledge. What is obligatory on the accountable person (al-mukallaf)? Al-Shaykh said: it is knowledge of Allah the Exalted because it is the basis of obligations. And al-Ustadh said: it is reflection on knowledge of Allah the Exalted for what has been mentioned of it being the prerequisite. And al-Qadi and al-Imam said: it is the intention to reflect, because reflection depends on it. The truth is that if what is meant is the first of the obligations intended in themselves, then it is knowledge; and if what is meant is the more general, then it is the intention to reflect, but its basis is on the obligation of the prerequisite of the absolute obligation (al-wajib al-mutlaq), and you have known what is in it. Therefore, he said in the text: 'otherwise, the second', meaning reflection or the intention to it.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage is a presentation of different opinions on the first obligation (al-wajib) for a person accountable before God. It begins by stating the disagreement: some say it is knowledge of God, because all other obligations are built upon it. Others say it is reflection (nazar) or the intention (qasd) to reflect, because knowledge depends on reflection. The correct view (al-haqq) distinguishes between two meanings of 'first obligation': if by that we mean what is sought for its own sake, then knowledge is first; if we mean the means to it, then reflection or the intention to reflect is first. The passage then explains that knowledge is not directly within one's power (maqdur), so it cannot be commanded as an act; rather, one must work towards it. It then mentions the view that doubt is the first obligation, because reflection follows doubt, but this is refuted on the grounds that doubt is a quality, not an act, and is not volitional. The passage then clarifies the obligation of the blind follower and the one with compound ignorance: they must reflect on the evidence to reach knowledge. It then cites the opinions of specific scholars: al-Shaykh (Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i?) holds knowledge; al-Ustadh (Abu Ishaq al-Isfara'ini?) holds reflection; al-Qadi (al-Baqillani) and al-Imam (al-Razi) hold intention to reflect. The passage concludes by affirming the correct position as per the matn: if the first obligation is understood as the intended end, it is knowledge; otherwise, it is reflection or intention to reflect. The commentary follows the author's order: statement of disagreement, presentation of each view with brief reasoning, the correct distinction, and the specific scholarly opinions.

الجهل المركب
الشيخ
الأستاذ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The muqallid is not required to reflect because he already follows someone.
Correction: The passage states that the muqallid and jahil murakkab must reflect on the evidence to reach knowledge; they cannot remain in blind faith.

Checkpoint
What is the first obligation for a person with compound ignorance (jahil murakkab) according to the passage?
A) Knowledge of Allah
B) Reflection (al-nazar) on evidence
C) Doubt
D) Intention to reflect

Key Takeaway
For the blind follower, the first obligation is reflection on evidence to attain knowledge, and the scholars' differing views are reconciled by the distinction between intended end and means.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite verses about Allah or hear His names, do you passively acknowledge them, or do you actively reflect (nazar) on the evidence they provide? Which scholar's view does your daily practice resemble?
?
The scholars debated whether the first obligation is knowledge or reflection. For you personally, is your pursuit of knowledge an end in itself, or do you ensure it is preceded by sincere intention and reflection? What would change if you prioritized reflection over mere accumulation?
?
If you have ever followed a scholar without understanding the evidence, this passage calls you a muqallid. Are you willing to examine one belief you hold about Allah this week through direct reflection on scripture and reason? What is the first step you will take?

Refuting Doubt as the First Obligation
Al-Taftazani refutes Abu Hashim's claim that doubt is the first wajib with two arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why doubt cannot be the first religious obligation according to al-Taftazani, and how he refutes Abu Hashim's argument using the distinction between volitional acts and passive qualities.

Core Idea
Doubt is a passive quality, not an act — it cannot be commanded as a duty.

Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i argued that the very first thing God requires of us is to doubt, because reflection leading to knowledge presupposes uncertainty. But how can doubt be an obligation if we cannot directly produce it? In this passage, al-Taftazani dissects this claim, showing that doubt is a passive state like knowledge, not an action under our control, and therefore cannot serve as a prerequisite for unconditional duty.

Guided Path
1
Understand the preliminary objection
Read the opening objection: someone might say simple ignorance should be the first obligation because reflection requires not already knowing. Notice the immediate reply: this ignorance is not volitional.
This sets up the key problem with grounding obligation in non-volitional states.
2
Recognize Abu Hashim's claim
Abu Hashim says doubt is the first obligation because intention to reflect depends on it. Identify the reasoning: you cannot intend to find an answer if you already have it.
3
Follow the first refutation
The first argument: doubt is a quality (kayfiyya) like knowledge, not directly under your power. It can be acquired or sustained, but that does not make it a prerequisite for obligation.
Al-Taftazani also refutes an objection from al-Mawaqif here.
4
Follow the second refutation
The second argument: the obligation of reflection itself is conditioned by doubt, so doubt cannot be a prerequisite for an unconditional (absolute) duty.
Doubt is compared to other non-volitional qualities like whiteness or blackness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يقال النظر مشروط بعدم المعرفة، بمعنى الجهل البسيط بالمطلوب، فينبغي أن يكون أول الواجبات، لأنا نقول هو ليس بمقدور بل حاصل قبل القدرة والإرادة، ولو سلم فوجوب النظر مقيد به لامتناع تحصيل الحاصل فلا يكون مقدمة للواجب المطلق واستدامته، وإن كانت مقدورة بأن ترك مباشرة أسباب حصول المعرفة لكنها ليست بمقدمة. وقال أبو هاشم أول الواجبات هو الشك لتوقف القصد إلى النظر عليه إذ لا بد من فهم الطرفين والنسبة مع عدم اعتقاد المطلوب أو نقيضه على ما سبق ورد بوجهين . أحدهما : أن الشك ليس بمقدور لكونه من الكيفيات كالعلم وإنما المقدور تحصيله أو استدامته بأن يحصل تصور الطرفين ويترك النظر في النسبة ولا شيء منهما بمقدمة، واعتراض المواقف بأنه لو لم يكن مقدوراً لم يكن العلم مقدوراً لأنه ضده ونسبة القدرة إلى الضدين على السواء ساقط، بما اعترف به من أن العلم ليس بمقدور، وإنما المقدور تحصيله بمباشرة الأسباب . وثانيهما : أن وجوب النظر والمعرفة مقيد بالشك، لما سبق من أنه لا إمكان للنظر بدونه فضلا عن الوجوب، فهو لا يكون مقدمة للواجب المطلق، بل كالعلم والجهل والقدرة والإرادة والبياض والسواد وشبه ذلك، وذلك أن القدرة إنما تتعلق بالأفعال الكسبيات لحركات والسكنات لا بالكيفيات.
It cannot be said: reflection is conditioned by the absence of knowledge, meaning simple ignorance of the desired thing, so it should be the first obligation. For we say: it is not within one's power, rather it occurs before capacity (al-qudra) and will (al-irada). And even if it is granted, the obligation of reflection is conditioned by it, because acquiring what is already obtained is impossible, so it is not a prerequisite for the absolute obligation (al-wajib al-mutlaq) nor for its continuance. And even if it were within one's power by refraining from directly seeking the causes of knowledge, it is still not a prerequisite. And Abu Hashim said: the first obligation is doubt, because the intention to reflect depends on it—for there must be understanding of the two sides and the relation, without believing the desired thing or its opposite, as previously mentioned. It was refuted with two points. The first: doubt is not within one's power because it is a quality (kayfiyya) like knowledge. Rather, what is within one's power is its acquisition or its continuance, by obtaining a conception of the two sides and refraining from reflection on the relation; none of these is a prerequisite. And the objection of [the author of] al-Mawaqif that if it were not within one's power then knowledge would not be within one's power because it is its opposite, and the capacity for opposites is equal, is invalid, given his admission that knowledge is not within one's power; rather, what is within one's power is its acquisition by directly seeking causes. The second: the obligation of reflection and knowledge is conditioned by doubt, as previously stated that reflection is impossible without it, let alone its obligation. Therefore, it is not a prerequisite for the absolute obligation. Rather, [it is] like knowledge, ignorance, capacity, will, whiteness, blackness, and the like. For capacity pertains only to acquisitive acts (al-af'al al-kasbiyya), i.e., movements and stillnesses, not to qualities.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the view that doubt (al-shakk) is the first obligation, as claimed by Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i. The passage first addresses a potential objection: one might argue that simple ignorance (al-jahl al-basit) should be the first obligation because reflection requires not already knowing the matter. The answer is that this ignorance is not within one's power—it occurs naturally before volition. Even if it were, the obligation of reflection is conditional upon it, so ignorance cannot be a universal prerequisite for the absolute obligation (al-wajib al-mutlaq). The passage then presents Abu Hashim's view: doubt is the first obligation because intention to reflect depends on doubt—one must not yet know the answer. The refutation consists of two arguments. The first argument: doubt is a quality (kayfiyya), like knowledge, and is not directly under one's power. What is under one's power is the acquisition of doubt or its continuance, such as by entertaining the two sides and refraining from reflection, but none of these is a prerequisite for obligation. An objection from al-Mawaqif that if doubt is not within one's power then knowledge is not either, because they are opposites and capacity extends equally to opposites, is dismissed by noting that knowledge is also not directly within one's power; only the means to knowledge are. The second argument: the obligation of reflection and knowledge is itself conditional upon doubt, as reflection is impossible without doubt. Thus, doubt cannot be a prerequisite for the absolute obligation (which is unconditional). The passage concludes by comparing doubt to other non-volitional qualities that are not objects of capacity—capacity only applies to acquired acts (kasbiyyat) like movements, not to qualities. The commentary follows the logical order: objection and answer, then Abu Hashim's claim, then the two refutations.

الكيف كالعلم

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that doubt can be commanded as an act, like moving one's hand.
Correction: The passage clarifies that doubt is a passive quality (kayfiyya), not a volitional act. Only the means to attain doubt (like considering both sides) can be commanded, but doubt itself is not under direct power.

Checkpoint
What is the first objection from al-Mawaqif that the passage refutes regarding the first point against Abu Hashim's view?
A) That doubt is within one's power because it is similar to knowledge
B) That if doubt is not within one's power, then knowledge would also not be within one's power, since they are opposites
C) That doubt is necessary for reflection and therefore must be obligatory
D) That doubt can be acquired through direct causes

Key Takeaway
Doubt is a passive quality that cannot be directly willed, so it cannot be the first religious obligation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever remained in a state of doubt about a fundamental belief, using the excuse that you are unsure rather than actively seeking the knowledge that would resolve it? If so, what are you waiting for?
?
Consider the qualities you possess—like certainty or doubt—do you act as if they are simply happen to you, or do you take responsibility for the means that lead to them? Which of your beliefs have you left to chance?
?
When you encounter a difficult question of faith, do you reflexively dismiss it as unanswerable, or do you engage in the reflection the scholars command? What is the cost of your avoidance?

The First Obligation: Knowledge or Intention to Reflect?
Taftazani distinguishes the first obligation as knowledge vs intention to reflect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between the first obligation intended per se (knowledge) and the broader sense (intention to reflect), and why this distinction matters in kalām.

Core Idea
The first obligation has two meanings: intrinsic knowledge and relative intention to reflect.

Al-Taftazani begins by presenting the view of al-Qadi and al-Imam, who held that the first obligation is the intention to reflect. However, he clarifies that this is only true if we consider obligation in a broad sense. In reality, the primary obligation intended for its own sake is knowledge of God. This distinction is crucial because it impacts how we understand the prerequisites for acquiring theological knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the two views presented.
2
Identify the distinction
Look for the phrase 'إن أريد أول الواجبات المقصودة بالذات فهو المعرفة' and 'إن أريد الأعم فهو القصد إلى النظر'. Understand the difference between intended per se and broader.
3
Connect to the broader context
See how this distinction relies on the principle of the obligation of prerequisites. Reflect on why this matters for kalām.
The commentary expands on this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال القاضي والإمام : هو القصد إلى النظر لتوقف النظر عليه (!)، والحق أنه إن أريد أول الواجبات المقصودة بالذات فهو المعرفة، وإن أريد الأعم فهو القصد إلى النظر، لكن مبناه على وجوب مقدمة الواجب المطلق، وقد عرفت ما فيه، فلذا قال في المتن وإلا فالثاني، أي النظر أو القصد إليه. لا يقال النظر مشروط بعدم المعرفة، بمعنى الجهل البسيط بالمطلوب، فينبغي أن يكون أول الواجبات، لأنا نقول هو ليس بمقدور بل حاصل قبل القدرة والإرادة، ولو سلم فوجوب النظر مقيد به لامتناع تحصيل الحاصل فلا يكون مقدمة للواجب المطلق واستدامته، وإن كانت مقدورة بأن ترك مباشرة أسباب حصول المعرفة لكنها ليست بمقدمة.
Al-Qadi and al-Imam said: It (the first obligation) is the intention to reflect, because reflection depends on it (!). The truth is that if what is meant is the first of the obligations intended per se, it is knowledge; if what is meant is the more general, it is the intention to reflect. But this is based on the obligation of the prerequisite of the absolute obligation, and you have known what is in it. Hence he said in the text: 'Otherwise, the second', meaning reflection or the intention to it. One cannot say: reflection is conditioned by absence of knowledge, meaning simple ignorance of the sought, so it should be the first obligation. For we say: it (reflection) is not within power but is obtained before ability and will. Even if it is granted, the obligation of reflection is restricted by it, because obtaining what is already obtained is impossible; thus it is not a prerequisite for the absolute obligation. And its continuance, even if it is within power by refraining from directly engaging in the causes of obtaining knowledge, is still not a prerequisite.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage defines the first obligation according to al-Qadi and al-Imam (al-Qadi Abd al-Jabbar and Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni). They state that the first obligation is the intention to reflect (al-qasd ila al-nazar), because reflection depends on this intention. However, the author (al-Taftazani) clarifies the truth: if we mean the first obligation intended for its own sake, it is knowledge (ma'rifa) of God; if we mean the first obligation in a broader sense, it is the intention to reflect. This distinction is based on the principle of the obligation of prerequisites (muqaddimat al-wajib al-mutlaq). The author then addresses a potential objection: one might think that reflection itself should be the first obligation because it is conditioned by the absence of knowledge (simple ignorance). The response is that reflection is not within human power (ghayr maqdur) but occurs naturally before ability and will. Even if it were within power, its obligation would be restricted by the condition of ignorance, making it a prerequisite for a conditioned obligation, not an absolute one. Continuing reflection, though possibly within power, is still not a prerequisite for the absolute obligation of acquiring knowledge. Thus the passage refines the concept of the first obligation by distinguishing between intrinsic and relative priority.

Key Terms
القصد إلى النظر
المعرفة
أول الواجبات
مقدمة الواجب المطلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the first obligation is the intention to reflect in an unqualified sense.
Correction: The first obligation intended for its own sake is knowledge; intention to reflect is only first in a relative sense as a prerequisite.

Checkpoint
According to al-Taftazani's clarification, what is the first obligation intended per se (bi al-dhat)?
A) Intention to reflect (al-qasd ila al-nazar)
B) Reflection (al-nazar)
C) Knowledge (al-ma'rifa)
D) Doubt (al-shakk)

Key Takeaway
The first obligation per se is knowledge; intention to reflect is only the first step under a broader definition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you claim to believe in God, is it because you have actively sought knowledge (ma'rifah) or because you merely intended to reflect but never followed through? Which of these does Taftazani say is the first obligation per se, and how does that challenge your own approach to faith?
?
The truth is that knowledge is the first obligation per se, and intention to reflect is only a broader sense. Have you ever conflated the two, thinking that merely intending to seek truth is enough without actually attaining knowledge? What concretely can you do today to move from intention to actual ma'rifah?
?
Consider the principle of 'obligation of the prerequisite' mentioned. Are you using the excuse of needing to do prerequisites (like learning methodology) to delay the actual obligation of knowing God? What is one step you have been avoiding that would bring you closer to the ma'rifah that is the true first duty?

Objection: Why Not Reflection as First Obligation?
Taftazani rebuts the objection that reflection should be the first obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why reflection cannot be the first obligation in an absolute sense due to its dependency on prior ignorance and its lack of voluntary control.

Core Idea
Reflection is conditioned by ignorance and beyond human will, so it cannot be an absolute first obligation.

Having established the distinction between the two senses of the first obligation, al-Taftazani now anticipates an objection. One might argue that reflection itself should be the first obligation because it logically requires the absence of knowledge (ignorance). He responds by showing that reflection is not within human power, and even if it were, its obligation would be conditional, not absolute. This clarifies why reflection cannot be the unqualified first obligation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Find the objection starting with 'لا يقال'. Understand what the objector is claiming.
The objection says reflection should be first because it requires simple ignorance.
2
Study the first response
Taftazani responds that reflection is not within human power. Why does that refute the objection?
3
Study the second response
Even if reflection were within power, it would be conditional. Why does that make it not a prerequisite for absolute obligation?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال القاضي والإمام : هو القصد إلى النظر لتوقف النظر عليه (!)، والحق أنه إن أريد أول الواجبات المقصودة بالذات فهو المعرفة، وإن أريد الأعم فهو القصد إلى النظر، لكن مبناه على وجوب مقدمة الواجب المطلق، وقد عرفت ما فيه، فلذا قال في المتن وإلا فالثاني، أي النظر أو القصد إليه. لا يقال النظر مشروط بعدم المعرفة، بمعنى الجهل البسيط بالمطلوب، فينبغي أن يكون أول الواجبات، لأنا نقول هو ليس بمقدور بل حاصل قبل القدرة والإرادة، ولو سلم فوجوب النظر مقيد به لامتناع تحصيل الحاصل فلا يكون مقدمة للواجب المطلق واستدامته، وإن كانت مقدورة بأن ترك مباشرة أسباب حصول المعرفة لكنها ليست بمقدمة.
Al-Qadi and al-Imam said: It (the first obligation) is the intention to reflect, because reflection depends on it (!). The truth is that if what is meant is the first of the obligations intended per se, it is knowledge; if what is meant is the more general, it is the intention to reflect. But this is based on the obligation of the prerequisite of the absolute obligation, and you have known what is in it. Hence he said in the text: 'Otherwise, the second', meaning reflection or the intention to it. One cannot say: reflection is conditioned by absence of knowledge, meaning simple ignorance of the sought, so it should be the first obligation. For we say: it (reflection) is not within power but is obtained before ability and will. Even if it is granted, the obligation of reflection is restricted by it, because obtaining what is already obtained is impossible; thus it is not a prerequisite for the absolute obligation. And its continuance, even if it is within power by refraining from directly engaging in the causes of obtaining knowledge, is still not a prerequisite.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage defines the first obligation according to al-Qadi and al-Imam (al-Qadi Abd al-Jabbar and Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni). They state that the first obligation is the intention to reflect (al-qasd ila al-nazar), because reflection depends on this intention. However, the author (al-Taftazani) clarifies the truth: if we mean the first obligation intended for its own sake, it is knowledge (ma'rifa) of God; if we mean the first obligation in a broader sense, it is the intention to reflect. This distinction is based on the principle of the obligation of prerequisites (muqaddimat al-wajib al-mutlaq). The author then addresses a potential objection: one might think that reflection itself should be the first obligation because it is conditioned by the absence of knowledge (simple ignorance). The response is that reflection is not within human power (ghayr maqdur) but occurs naturally before ability and will. Even if it were within power, its obligation would be restricted by the condition of ignorance, making it a prerequisite for a conditioned obligation, not an absolute one. Thus the passage refines the concept of the first obligation by distinguishing between intrinsic and relative priority.

Misconception & Correction

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever realized that your moments of genuine reflection are not entirely within your control? Does this humble you or make you anxious? How does this affect your view of seeking truth?
?
If reflection cannot be commanded as a first duty, then what is your actual obligation regarding the use of your intellect? Are you simply waiting for reflection to happen, or are you actively preparing the conditions for it?
?
The passage says reflection arises 'before ability and will.' When was the last time you allowed yourself to pause and reflect without forcing it? What distractions do you need to remove to make space for natural reflection on your beliefs?

Abu Hashim's Claim: The First Obligation is Doubt (First Refutation)
Al-Taftazani presents Abu Hashim's claim and the first refutation with an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu Hashim's claim that doubt is the first obligation and the first refutation based on doubt not being within human power.

Core Idea
Doubt is not a commanded act; it is a passive state, not a chosen action.

Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i, a leading Mu'tazili, argued that before one can reflect, one must first doubt. But is doubt something we can be commanded to do? The first refutation challenges the very premise that doubt is within our control. This lesson introduces the claim and its initial critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: claim, rationale, then refutation with objection and response.
Pay attention to the term 'al-shakk' (doubt) and how it is defined later.
2
Identify Abu Hashim's claim and its justification
Find the sentence stating that the first obligation is doubt. Understand his reasoning: doubt is necessary to set the intention to reflect, because reflection requires having the two sides of a proposition in mind without believing either.
This is based on the idea that you cannot seek something you already know.
3
Analyze the first refutation
The first refutation argues that doubt is not within power (ghayr maqdur) because it is a quality (kayfiyya) like knowledge. Only its acquisition or continuation is within power, but neither is a prerequisite for the absolute obligation.
Contrast this with acts like prayer, which are directly chosen.
4
Understand the objection from al-Mawaqif and its dismissal
The objection claimed that if doubt were not in power, then knowledge would also not be in power since they are opposites. The dismissal states that knowledge itself is not in power either; only its acquisition is, so the objection fails.
This shows the consistency of the refuter's position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال أبو هاشم أول الواجبات هو الشك (٢) لتوقف القصد إلى النظر عليه (۳) إذ لا بد من فهم الطرفين والنسبة مع عدم اعتقاد المطلوب أو نقيضه على ما سبق ورد بوجهين .
أحدهما : أن الشك ليس بمقدور لكونه من الكيفيات كالعلم وإنما المقدور تحصيله أو استدامته بأن يحصل تصور الطرفين ويترك النظر في النسبة ولا شيء منهما بمقدمة، واعتراض المواقف (٤) بأنه لو لم يكن مقدوراً لم يكن العلم مقدوراً لأنه ضده ونسبة القدرة إلى الضدين على السواء ساقط، بما اعترف به من أن العلم ليس بمقدور، وإنما المقدور تحصيله بمباشرة الأسباب .
وثانيهما : أن وجوب النظر والمعرفة مقيد بالشك، لما سبق من أنه لا إمكان للنظر بدونه فضلا عن الوجوب، فهو لا يكون مقدمة للواجب المطلق، بل للمقيد به كالنصاب للزكاة والاستطاعة للحج، فلا يجب تحصيله، ولما كان (۱) إيجاب المعرفة هو إيجاب النظر . قال في المواقف : إن وجوب المعرفة مقيد بالشك، وإلا فالقول بوجوب الشك إنما يبنى (۲) على كونه مقدمة للنظر، لا للمعرفة وكلا الوجهين ضعيف .
أما الأول : فلأنهم لا يعنون بمقدورية مقدمة الواجب أن يكون من الأفعال الاختيارية، بل أن يتمكن المكلف من تحصيله كالطهارة للصلاة وملك النصاب للزكاة، ومعنى وجوبها، وجوب تحصيلها.
وأما الثاني : فلأنه يقتضي أن لا يجب النظر والمعرفة عند الوهم، أو الظن، أو التقليد أو الجهل المركب وفساده بين ويمكن دفع الوهم، والظن بأن الشك الشك يتناولهما لأن معناه التردد في النسبة (۳) إما على استواء وهـو المحض أو رجحان لأحد الجانبين وهو الظن والوهم، ودفع التقليد، والجهل المركب بأن الواجب معها هو النظر في الدليل، ومعرفة وجـه دلالته ليؤلا إلى العلم ، وذلك لأن امتناع النظر والطلب عند الجزم بالمطلوب أو نقيضه مما لم يقع فيه نزاع، وقد يقال في رد الشك المحض (٤) أنه وإن كان مقدمة للنظر الواجب فليس من أسبابه ليكون إيجابه إيجاباً له بمعنى تعلق خطاب الشارع به وفيه نظر، لأن مراد أبي هاشم هو الوجوب العقلي كالنظر والمعرفة . نعم لو قيل : إنه ليس من المعاني التي يطلبها العاقل، ويحكم باستحقاق تاركه الذم لكان شيئاً . وستعرف فساد النظر بمعرفة معنى الوجوب العقلي (٥) .
Abu Hashim said: The first obligation is doubt (al-shakk), because the intention to reflect depends on it, since one must understand the two sides and the relation without belief in the sought or its opposite, as previously mentioned. It is refuted in two aspects. The first: that doubt is not within power because it is among the qualities like knowledge; rather, what is within power is its acquisition or its continuation, that is, by obtaining the conception of the two sides and refraining from reflection on the relation, and neither of these is a prerequisite. And the objection of al-Mawaqif that if it were not within power, then knowledge would not be within power because it (doubt) is its opposite, and the relation of power to opposites is equal, is fallen, given the admission that knowledge is not within power; rather, what is within power is its acquisition by directly engaging the causes. The second: that the obligation of reflection and knowledge is conditioned by doubt, because as previously mentioned, there is no possibility of reflection without it, let alone obligation. Thus it (doubt) is not a prerequisite for the absolute obligation, but rather for that which is conditioned by it, like the nisab for zakat and the ability (istita'a) for hajj; therefore its acquisition is not obligatory. And since making knowledge obligatory is making reflection obligatory, he said in al-Mawaqif: 'The obligation of knowledge is conditioned by doubt; otherwise, the claim that doubt is obligatory is based on it being a prerequisite for reflection, not for knowledge, and both aspects are weak.' As for the first: because they (the opponents) do not mean by 'being within power' of a prerequisite that it be among voluntary acts, but rather that the legally responsible person is able to acquire it, like purification for prayer and possession of the nisab for zakat, and the meaning of its obligation is the obligation to acquire it. As for the second: because it requires that reflection and knowledge would not be obligatory in cases of wahm (conjecture), zann (opinion), taqlid (blind following), or jahl murakkab (compounded ignorance), and its corruption is evident. And one can repel wahm and zann by saying that doubt encompasses them, because its meaning is hesitation regarding the relation, either equally—which is pure doubt (al-shakk al-mahd)—or with preponderance of one side, which is zann and wahm. And one can repel taqlid and jahl murakkab by saying that what is obligatory along with them is reflection on the evidence and knowledge of the aspect of its indication, so that it leads to knowledge. This is because the impossibility of reflection and seeking when one is certain of the sought or its opposite is not disputed. And it may be said in refutation of pure doubt that even if it is a prerequisite for obligatory reflection, it is not among its causes, such that making it obligatory would be making reflection obligatory in the sense of the address of the Lawgiver attaching to it. But there is consideration in this, because Abu Hashim intended rational obligation (al-wujub al-'aqli) like reflection and knowledge. Yes, if it were said that it is not among the meanings that a rational person seeks and judges the one who abandons it to be blameworthy, that would be something. And you will come to know the corruption of reflection by knowing the meaning of rational obligation.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage presents Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i's claim that the first obligation is doubt (al-shakk), because the intention to reflect depends on doubt—one must understand the two sides of the proposition and the relation between them, without holding belief in the sought or its opposite. The author then gives two refutations of this view. The first refutation: doubt is not within human power (ghayr maqdur) because it is a quality (kayfiyya) like knowledge; only the acquisition or continuation of doubt is within power, but neither acquisition nor continuation is a prerequisite (muqaddima) for the absolute obligation. The author notes an objection from al-Mawaqif that if doubt were not within power, then knowledge also would not be within power since they are opposites, but this objection is dismissed because it is admitted that knowledge itself is not within power—only its acquisition is. The second refutation: the obligation of reflection and knowledge is conditioned by doubt, so doubt is a prerequisite for a conditioned obligation, not for the absolute obligation, similar to the nisab for zakat or ability (istita'a) for hajj; thus acquiring doubt is not obligatory. The author then quotes al-Mawaqif's response that both refutations are weak. He explains why the first is weak: the opponents (refuters) do not mean by 'being within power' that the prerequisite must be a voluntary act, but rather that the mukallaf is able to acquire it, like purification for prayer (which may not be voluntary in every detail but is still acquirable). The second refutation is weak because it would imply that reflection and knowledge are not obligatory in cases of wahm, zann, taqlid, or jahl murakkab, which is false. Wahm and zann are included under doubt because the meaning of shakk is hesitation (taraddud) in the relation, either equal (pure doubt) or with preponderance (zann/wahm). Taqlid and jahl murakkab are addressed by saying that the obligation in those states is to reflect on evidence to reach knowledge, since reflection is impossible when one is certain. Finally, a further point: even if pure doubt is a prerequisite for reflection, it is not a cause, so making it obligatory would not directly oblige reflection—but this is considered, because Abu Hashim means rational obligation (wujub 'aqli), not legal obligation. The passage ends with a note that the corruption of this view will be understood later.

Key Terms
الشك
النظر
المعرفة
المقدورية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That doubt is a commanded act like prayer or fasting.
Correction: The passage clarifies that doubt is a passive quality, not a voluntary action, so it cannot be directly obligated.

Checkpoint
According to the first refutation, why is doubt not considered to be within human power?
A) because it is a quality like knowledge
B) because it is a voluntary act
C) because it leads to knowledge
D) because it is required for reflection

Key Takeaway
Doubt is not a commanded act; it is a passive state that one cannot be directly obligated to produce.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever excused yourself from the duty to seek knowledge of Allah by saying you are in a state of doubt? This lesson shows that doubt is not a commanded state—it is not even within your power. What concrete action will you take this week to begin reflecting on the evidence for His existence, despite your feelings?
?
The refutation concedes that knowledge itself is not directly under your control; only its acquisition through means is. When you pray for knowledge, do you also take the means—reading, studying, asking scholars? What means will you commit to starting tomorrow, without waiting for a perfect state of certainty?
?
When you face an argument in aqeedah, do you use doubt as a shield to avoid intellectual responsibility? The passage teaches that doubt is a passive quality, not an active command. Are you using doubt to procrastinate on your obligation to reflect? Decide now: what is the first step you will take to engage with the proofs of tawheed?

The Second Refutation and Its Critique
Al-Taftazani presents the second refutation and the rebuttals from al-Mawaqif.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second refutation of Abu Hashim's claim and the counter-arguments that show its weakness.

Core Idea
Obligations conditioned on doubt make doubt a prerequisite, not an absolute duty.

The second refutation argues that even if doubt is necessary for reflection, it is only a condition for a conditioned obligation, not an absolute one. But critics respond that this would exempt many from the duty to know Allah. This lesson explores the back-and-forth.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the second refutation
Locate the second refutation: it states that the obligation of reflection and knowledge is conditioned by doubt, so doubt is like the nisab for zakat—a prerequisite, not itself obligatory.
Note the analogy with zakat and hajj.
2
Understand the response from al-Mawaqif
Al-Mawaqif responds that both refutations are weak. The passage then gives two reasons why the first refutation is weak: they misunderstand 'being within power' as requiring voluntary action, when it only means being able to acquire it.
Example: purification for prayer may not be voluntary in detail, but it is acquirable.
3
Examine the critique of the second refutation
The second refutation is weak because it would imply that reflection and knowledge are not obligatory in cases of conjecture, opinion, blind following, or compounded ignorance, which is false.
This shows the refutation leads to unacceptable consequences.
