The Final Answer: Two Meanings of Correspondence
The author gives a detailed answer clarifying the possible meanings of correspondence and rejecting the objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's final resolution by distinguishing two meanings of 'correspondence' and how the intellect can abstract multiple images from one external identity.

Core Idea
Correspondence does not require one-to-one external distinctness; the mind can abstract many concepts from one thing.

The final answer resolves the objection by clarifying what 'correspondence' means. The author distinguishes between two interpretations: one where each mental image has a separate external identity, and another where a single external identity corresponds to multiple images. He argues that ignorance would only follow in the first interpretation if the images are taken as representations of distinct external things. In the second interpretation, correspondence is possible without external distinctness because the intellect abstracts different concepts from one reality through different considerations.

Guided Path
1
Read the two meanings of correspondence
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Identify the two distinct definitions of correspondence that the author proposes.
The first is one-to-one, the second is one-to-many.
2
Understand why each avoids ignorance
For each interpretation, the author explains why ignorance does not necessarily follow. Note the condition that would lead to ignorance.
Focus on the phrase 'إنما يلزم لو أخذت في الذهن على أنها صور لأمور متمايزة في الخارج'.
3
Appreciate the role of different considerations
The second interpretation relies on the intellect abstracting multiple images from one thing through different considerations (i'tibarat). This is a key concept in kalam metaphysics.
The author promises further clarification in the discussion of quiddity.

Distinguishing Things with and without Mental Identity
Author explains logical distinction between identity in essence and identity in conception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how things without fixed mental identity can still be conceptually distinguished, avoiding common fallacies in reasoning.

Core Idea
Non-identity in essence can still have identity in conception.

This passage addresses a subtle logical puzzle: how can we talk about things that are not fixed in the mind or even inconceivable? The author explains that such things have no identity in terms of essence, but they do have identity in terms of being conceived. This distinction helps prevent fallacies when reasoning about existence and conception.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the key divisions
Notice how the author divides things into conceivable/inconceivable and fixed/not-fixed in the mind. Look for the phrase 'ممكن التصور' and 'لاممكن التصور'.
The author uses a series of comparisons.
3
Understand the puzzle
Recognize the apparent contradiction: how can non-identity (لا هوية) be a counterpart of identity (هوية)? Reflect on why this seems problematic.
The key is the distinction between essence (ذات) and conception (تصور).
4
Follow the resolution
See how the author resolves the puzzle by clarifying that things have no identity in essence but do have identity in conception. The example of identity and non-identity illustrates this.
The resolution is that there is no contradiction when the two perspectives are distinguished.
5
Apply the principle
Consider how this principle helps to avoid fallacies. Why is it important to separate essence from conception in logical reasoning?
This principle is foundational for preventing errors in metaphysical discussions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من الدات وقسما منه باعتبار كونه متصوراً، وكذا في تقسيمه إلى ممكن التصور، واللاممكن التصور، فيكون الثاني قسماً ممكن التصور، بل من المتصور له أن يحكم بالتمايز بين الثابت في الذهن، واللاثابت فيه، وكذا بين ممكن التصور، واللاممكن التصور، مع أنه يستدعي أن يكون للممتازين هویتان عند العقل ولا هوية للاثابت في العقل واللاممكن التصور، فيكون كل منهما لا هوية له عند العقل، من حيث الذات، وله هوية عنده من حيث التصور، وهذا كما أنه يعتبر الهوية واللاهوية ويحكم بينهما بالتمايز، فتكون اللاهوية قسيماً للهوية بحسب الذات وقسماً منها باعتبار ثبوتها في العقل، ولا تناقض في شيء من ذلك، وهذه أصول يستعان بها على حال كثير من المغالطة.
From the essence, and a part of it in terms of being conceivable, and likewise in its division into the conceivable and the inconceivable, so the second is a part of the conceivable. Rather, it is within the conceivable to judge the distinction between what is fixed in the mind and what is not fixed in it, and likewise between the conceivable and the inconceivable, even though this requires that the distinguished things have two identities in the mind, while there is no identity for the not-fixed in the mind and the inconceivable. Thus each of them has no identity in the mind in terms of essence, yet has identity in it in terms of conception. This is like when one considers identity and non-identity and judges between them by distinction, so non-identity becomes a counterpart of identity in terms of essence and a part of it in terms of its being established in the mind. There is no contradiction in any of that, and these are principles that help in resolving many fallacies.
Source pages p. 387

Commentary
This passage discusses a subtle logical and metaphysical distinction: how things that have no fixed existence in the mind or are impossible to conceive can still be meaningfully discussed. The author explains that when we divide things into conceivable and inconceivable, the inconceivable is nonetheless a part of the conceivable (as a concept). Similarly, we can judge a distinction between what is fixed in the mind and what is not, even though the not-fixed has no identity (huwiyya) in the mind from the perspective of essence. However, it does have an identity from the perspective of conception (tasawwur). The example of considering identity (huwiyya) and non-identity (lahuwiyya) is given: non-identity is a counterpart of identity in essence but also a part of it when considered as established in the mind. This is not contradictory. These principles help avoid fallacies in reasoning.

Key Terms
هوية (identity)
لا هوية (non-identity)
متصور (conceived)
ثابت في الذهن (fixed in the mind)
لاثابت (not fixed)
ممكن التصور (possible to conceive)
لاممكن التصور (impossible to conceive)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That things without identity in the mind cannot be distinguished at all.
Correction: The author shows that while they lack identity in essence, they have identity in conception, allowing meaningful distinction.

Checkpoint
What does the author say about things that are not fixed in the mind or impossible to conceive?
A) They have no identity at all.
B) They have identity in terms of conception but not in essence.
C) They cannot be distinguished.
D) They are the same as fixed things.

Key Takeaway
Things without mental identity in essence can still be distinguished through their identity in conception, avoiding logical fallacies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed something as 'inconceivable' in your understanding of Allah's attributes, closing yourself off from deeper reflection? How does this lesson challenge you to reconsider your own mental categories and remain open to what your mind can still conceive?
?
When you encounter apparent contradictions in your faith, do you immediately assume they are real contradictions, or do you pause to examine whether the distinction lies in essence vs conception? How can you apply this principle right now to a specific doubt or confusion you face?
?
Think of a time you judged something as 'non-existent' in your life and stopped considering it. Could it be that it still has identity in your mind and could be distinguished? What does that mean for your ability to reason about the unseen, and how will you change the way you think about what you cannot see?

The True Criterion for Judgment
Sanusi defines truth as correspondence to nafs al-amr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the truth of a judgment cannot depend on correspondence to external objects or mental ideas, but must refer to 'the thing itself' (nafs al-amr).

Core Idea
Truth is not about matching reality or thoughts—it is about matching the thing as it is in itself.

Sanusi begins by establishing the criterion for a correct judgment. He rejects two common views: that truth requires external existence, and that truth is simply agreement with mental representations. Instead, he anchors truth in the concept of 'nafs al-amr'—the thing considered independently of any observer, judge, or informant.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get a sense of the argument. Notice the clear rejection of two alternative criteria before the positive definition.
2
Identify the two rejected criteria
Find where Sanusi says truth is NOT correspondence to external entities (aʿyān) and NOT merely correspondence to mental representations (adhān). Underline each rejection.
3
Understand the positive criterion
Focus on the phrase 'mā fī nafs al-amr' and its explanation: 'the thing as it is in itself, disregarding the judgment of the judge.' Reflect on how this differs from the two rejected views.
Note the dismissal of interpreting nafs al-amr as the Active Intellect—this is a technical point showing the concept is more basic.
4
Consider examples
Think of an example like 'the conjunction of two opposites is impossible.' Does this statement correspond to any external object or mental image? It matches the real nature of impossibility, even though impossibles have no existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إثبات صحة الحكم
قال : ولا يشترط في صحة المطابقة لما في الأعيان ، إذ قد لا يوجد فيها الطرفان، ولا يكفي المطابقة لما في الأذهان، اذ قد يرتسم فيها الكواذب، بل المعتبر المطابقة لما في نفس الأمر، ومعناه ما يفهم من قولنا هذا الأمر كذا في نفسه أي في حد ذاته مع قطع النظر عن حكم الحاكم وتفسيره بما في العقل الفعال بعيداً جداً، اذا قصد بيان المفهوم، لأنه قد يقع التصديق ممن لا يعرفه بل ينكره وأما الاعتراض بأنه لا يشمل علمه، ولا العلم السابق عليه، ولا العلم بالجزئيات فيمكن دفعه : . يعني . أن الحكم قد يكون صحيحاً أي حقاً وصدقاً وقد يكون فاسداً أي باطلاً وكذباً وإن كان غالب استعمال الصدق والكذب في الأقوال خاصة، وليست صحة الحكم بمطابقته لما في الأعيان، إذ قد لا يتحقق طرفا الحكم في الخارج، كما في الحكم بالأمور الذهنية، على الأمور الذهنية أو الخارجية . كقولنا : الإمكان اعتباري ومقابل للامتناع واجتماع النقيضين ممتنع .
كقولنا : الإنسان ممكن أو أعمى ولا يكفي المطابقة لما في الأذهان لأنه قد يرتسم فيها الأحكام الغير المطابقة للواقع ، فلزم أن يكون قولنا : العالم قديم حقاً وصدقاً، لمطابقته لما في أذهان الفلاسفة، وهو باطل قطعاً، بل المعتبر في صحة الحكم مطابقته لما في نفس الأمر، وهو المراد بـالـواقـع والخارج، أي خارج ذات المدرك والمخبر ومعناه ما يفهم من قولنا هذا الأمر كذا في نفسه أو ليس كذا أي في حد ذاته وبالنظر إليه مع قطع النظر عن إدراك المدرك ، وإخبار المخبر على أن المراد بالأمر الشأن والشيء، وبالنفس الذات .
فإن قيل : كيف يتصور هذا فيما لا ذات له، ولا شيئية في الأعيان كالمعدومات سيما الممتنعات . فالجواب إجمالاً : أنا نعلم قطعاً أن قولنا : اجتماع الضدين مستحيل مطابق لما في نفس الأمر وقولنا : إنه ممكن غير مطابق، وإن لم يعلم كيفية
Establishing the correctness of judgment. He said: Correctness of judgment does not require correspondence to what is in external entities, since the two terms may not exist in them; nor is correspondence to what is in minds sufficient, since falsehoods may be depicted in them. Rather the criterion is correspondence to what is in the matter itself (nafs al-amr), and its meaning is what is understood from our saying 'this matter is such in itself', i.e., in its own essence, disregarding the judgment of the judge. And interpreting it as the Active Intellect is very far-fetched when intending to explain the concept, because assent may occur from one who does not know it but denies it. As for the objection that it does not encompass His knowledge, nor prior knowledge, nor knowledge of particulars, it can be refuted... meaning that the judgment may be correct, i.e., true and veracious, and may be false, i.e., invalid and false, although the common usage of truth and falsehood is especially in statements. And the correctness of a judgment is not due to its correspondence to external entities, since the two terms of the judgment may not be realized externally, as in judgments about mental matters upon mental or external matters, like our saying: possibility is an attribute and is opposite to impossibility, and the conjunction of two contradictories is impossible. Like our saying: man is possible or blind. Nor is correspondence to what is in minds sufficient, because judgments that do not correspond to reality may be depicted in them, so it would follow that our saying 'the world is eternal' is true and veracious because of its correspondence to what is in the minds of the philosophers, yet it is absolutely false. Rather the criterion in the correctness of a judgment is its correspondence to what is in the matter itself (nafs al-amr), and that is what is meant by 'reality' (al-wāqiʿ) and 'external' (al-khārij), i.e., external to the perceiver's essence and the informant. Its meaning is what is understood from our saying 'this matter is such in itself' or 'is not such', i.e., in its own essence and considering it without regard to the perception of the perceiver or the report of the reporter, such that what is meant by 'matter' (al-amr) is affair/thing, and by 'self' (al-nafs) is essence (al-dhāt). If it is said: How is this conceivable for what has no essence and no thingness in external entities, like non-existents especially impossibles? The answer in summary: We know with certainty that our saying 'the conjunction of two opposites is impossible' corresponds to what is in the matter itself, and our saying 'it is possible' does not correspond, even if we do not know the modality.
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
This passage defines the criterion for the truth (ṣiḥḥa) of a judgment (ḥukm). The author begins by rejecting two inadequate criteria. First, truth does not require correspondence (muṭābaqa) to external entities (aʿyān), because judgments often involve terms that have no external existence (e.g., mental concepts). Second, correspondence to mental representations (adhān) is insufficient, because false beliefs exist in minds (e.g., philosophers' belief that the world is eternal). The correct criterion is correspondence to 'what is in the matter itself' (nafs al-amr), which means the thing considered in itself, independent of any judge, perceiver, or informant. The author dismisses an interpretation linking this to the Active Intellect (ʿaql faʿʿāl) as far-fetched, since the concept should be understandable even to those who deny that. He then addresses a potential objection about judgments regarding non-existents (maʿdūmāt) and impossibles (mumtaniʿāt). The reply is that we know with certainty that statements like 'the conjunction of two opposites is impossible' correspond to reality (nafs al-amr), even if we cannot explain how. This shows that truth is not limited to external existence but applies to the intrinsic nature of things.

Key Terms
ṣiḥḥat al-ḥukm (correctness of judgment)
muṭābaqa (correspondence)
nafs al-amr (the thing itself)
wāqiʿ (reality)
khārij (external)
ʿaql faʿʿāl (Active Intellect)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that truth of a judgment is determined by its correspondence to external objects or to mental representations.
Correction: Sanusi argues that both are insufficient: judgments can be true without external existence (e.g., about mental concepts) and false beliefs can exist in minds. Truth is correspondence to the thing itself (nafs al-amr).

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject correspondence to mental representations (adhān) as the criterion for truth?
A) Because mental representations are always false
B) Because false judgments can exist in minds
C) Because the mind cannot perceive external objects
D) Because mental representations are subjective

Key Takeaway
Truth of a judgment is determined by its correspondence to the thing as it is in itself (nafs al-amr), not by external existence or mental agreement.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there judgments you hold dear that you have never questioned against the standard of nafs al-amr? What would it cost you to examine them today?
?
When you assert something as true, do you rely on the agreement of others or on the thing as it is in itself? Can you name one belief you hold solely because it is popular among people you respect?
?
If you knew that every human mind held a falsehood, would you still have the courage to stand with the truth of nafs al-amr, even if it meant standing alone? What is the first step you will take to align your heart with that truth?

Applying Nafs al-Amr to Non-Existents
Sanusi answers an objection about nafs al-amr regarding non-existents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the criterion of nafs al-amr applies to judgments about non-existents (maʿdūmāt) and impossibles (mumtaniʿāt).

Core Idea
Truth does not require existence—even impossibles have a real nature that makes some statements about them true.

A potential objection to the nafs al-amr criterion is that non-existents and impossibles have no essence to correspond to. How can a judgment about something that does not exist be true? Sanusi responds that we know with certainty that statements like 'the conjunction of two opposites is impossible' are true, even if we cannot fully explain the modality of this correspondence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objection carefully: 'How can this be conceived for what has no essence and no thingness in external entities, like non-existents, especially impossibles?' Understand the challenge.
The objection targets the applicability of nafs al-amr to things that do not exist externally.
2
Study the answer
Sanusi's answer is brief: we know with certainty that some statements about impossibles are true, even if we do not know the 'how' of this correspondence.
The answer relies on direct certainty (ḍarūra) rather than a detailed explanation.
3
Reflect on the implication
Think about what this means for the nature of truth: truth is not limited to existence but pertains to the real natures of things, even those that cannot exist.

Source Text

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are certain of some truths about Allah—His oneness, His power—yet you cannot fully grasp how they are true. When you feel the need to ‘explain’ before you submit, are you not like the objector who demands a how? Will you today choose to stand firm on your certainty, even without full understanding?
?
This passage says: ‘We know with certainty even if we do not know the modality.’ Is there a single Islamic belief you hold that you cannot logically prove from scratch? If you lost the ability to defend it intellectually, would your certainty remain? What would that mean for your trust in Allah’s revelation over your own mind?
?
The objector thought truth requires a subject. Do you ever limit Allah's truth to what you can conceive or experience? Commit now to affirming one truth about Allah that seems impossible to your reason—without needing to understand how it fits—just as Sanusi affirms the impossibility of opposites.

Refutation of Identifying 'What is in the Mind' with the Active Intellect
Al-Taftazani refutes the identification of 'ma fi nafs al-amr' with the Active Intellect and answers an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Taftazani's refutation of equating 'what is in the mind' with the Active Intellect, and his response to a key objection.

Core Idea
The nature of truth in judgment does not depend on the Active Intellect.

Al-Taftazani here tackles a philosophical identification made by some thinkers who claim that the standard of truth ('what is in the mind') is actually the Active Intellect. He argues that this view is both scripturally and rationally unsound. Moreover, even if one tries to defend it, the notion of correspondence falls apart. Let’s trace his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the author's critical tone and the structure of refutation.
2
Identify the main claim being refuted
Find where al-Taftazani states that the identification is 'absolutely false'. Note his first argument about common knowledge.
He uses the simple judgment 'one is half of two' as an example.
3
Understand the objection
The passage presents a possible objection: if we identify 'what is in the mind' with the Active Intellect, correspondence fails. Identify the three parts of this objection.
They concern duality, God's knowledge, and particulars.
4
Understand the answer given
Al-Taftazani briefly answers the first part. How does he respond? What does it mean for a judgment to be 'the very same thing'?
This points to a different theory of truth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال : وهو الذي عبر عنه في القرآن المجيد باللوح المحفوظ والكتاب المبين، المشتمل على كل رطب ويابس وأنت خبير بأن ما ذكره مع ضعف بعض مقدماته مخالف لصريح قوله تعالى : (وعنده مفاتح الغيب) الآية . فليته سكت عن التطبيق، ثم القول بأن المراد بما في نفس الأمر، ما في العقل الفعال باطل قطعاً ، لأن كل أحد من العقلاء يعرف أن قولنا : الواحد نصف الاثنين مطابق لما نفس الأمر، أنه لم يتصور العقل الفعال أصلاً، مع فضلا عن اعتقاد ثبوته، وارتسامه بصور الكائنات ، بلى مع أنه ينكر ثبوته، ويعتقد انتفاءه على ما هو رأي المتكلمين وكان المراد أن ما في نفس الأمر على وجه يعم الكل، ولا يحتمل النقيض أصلاً، هو ما في العقل الفعال وإن تغايرا بحسب المفهوم، وقد يقال : لو أريد بما في نفس الأمر في علم العقل الفعال امتنع اعتبار المطابقة لما في نفس الأمر في علم العقل الفعال لعدم الاثنينية، وفي العلم السابق عليه ولو بالذات كعلم الواجب لامتناع مطابقة الشيء لما لا تحقق له معه وفي العلم بالجزئيات مثل هذا الحرف، وقيام زيد في هذا الوقت لامتناع ارتسامها في العقل ويمكن الجواب عن الأول : بأن صحة الحكم الذي في نفس الأمر لا يكون لكونه مطابقا لما في نفس الأمر بل عينه.
Then he said: And it is that which is referred to in the Noble Qur’an as the Preserved Tablet (al-lawḥ al-maḥfūẓ) and the Clear Book (al-kitāb al-mubīn), containing every moist and dry. And you are well aware that what he mentioned, despite the weakness of some of its premises, contradicts the explicit statement of God Most High: 'And with Him are the keys of the unseen' (Qur’an 6:59) to the end of the verse. Would that he had kept silent about the application. Then the statement that what is meant by 'what is in the mind' (mā fī nafs al-amr) is what is in the Active Intellect is absolutely false, because every rational person knows that our saying 'one is half of two' corresponds to what is in the mind, while he has never conceived the Active Intellect at all, let alone believing in its existence and the imprinting of forms of beings in it. Indeed, even while he denies its existence and believes in its negation according to the view of the theologians (al-mutakallimūn). And it was meant that what is in the mind in a way that includes everything and does not admit of opposite at all is what is in the Active Intellect, even if they differ in concept. And it might be said: If what is meant by 'what is in the mind' is what is in the knowledge of the Active Intellect, then the consideration of correspondence to what is in the mind in the knowledge of the Active Intellect would be impossible due to the absence of duality. And in the knowledge prior to it, even if by essence, like the knowledge of the Necessary, it is impossible for a thing to correspond to what has no realization with it. And with regard to knowledge of particulars, such as 'this letter' and 'Zayd's standing at this time', it is impossible due to the impossibility of their imprinting in the intellect. And the answer to the first can be given: that the correctness of the judgment that is in the mind is not because it corresponds to what is in the mind, but it is the very same thing.
Source pages p. 394

Commentary
This passage presents al-Taftazani's refutation of the identification of 'what is in the mind' (mā fī nafs al-amr) with the Active Intellect (al-ʿaql al-faʿʿāl), along with an objection and its answer. The author first quotes someone (probably the original matn) who claims that the Active Intellect is what the Qur’an calls the Preserved Tablet and the Clear Book. Then al-Taftazani criticizes this identification, stating that it contradicts the Qur’anic verse 'And with Him are the keys of the unseen' (6:59). He wishes the author had refrained from this application. Then he asserts that the claim is absolutely false because any rational person knows that a simple judgment like 'one is half of two' corresponds to what is in the mind without having any concept of the Active Intellect, let alone believing in it. This is especially clear for theologians who deny its existence. Next, he presents an objection: if 'what is in the mind' is identified with the knowledge of the Active Intellect, then the notion of correspondence would break down because there would be no duality (the judgment and the object would be identical). Similarly, if referred to God's knowledge, a judgment cannot correspond to something that has no reality in that knowledge (since God's knowledge is the source, not a copy). And for particulars, they cannot be imprinted in the Active Intellect because it is immaterial and particular. Then al-Taftazani provides an answer to the first part of the objection: the correctness of the judgment that is in the mind is not because it corresponds to something else in the mind; rather, it is that very thing itself. This suggests that the judgment is self-identical to the reality, not a correspondence to an external copy.

Key Terms
لوح محفوظ
كتاب مبين
عقل فعال
ما في نفس الأمر
مطابقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'what is in the mind' (the standard of truth) is identical to the Active Intellect.
Correction: Al-Taftazani shows this is false because ordinary judgments do not require the Active Intellect, and it contradicts both scripture and the notion of correspondence.

Checkpoint
What is the first part of the objection against identifying 'what is in the mind' with the Active Intellect?
A) The Active Intellect is not eternal
B) Correspondence would be impossible due to lack of duality
C) The Active Intellect cannot contain multiplicity
D) The Qur’an denies the Active Intellect

Key Takeaway
Al-Taftazani argues that truth judgments stand independent of the Active Intellect, and the identification leads to contradictions that even his answer only partially resolves.

The Five Sections of the Third Chapter
Outline of the third chapter's five sections

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five topics that comprise the chapter on the attachments of existence and essence.

Core Idea
The chapter's five sections form a roadmap for analyzing existence and essence.

This passage presents the structure of the third chapter of the text, listing the five key topics that will be discussed. It serves as a roadmap for understanding the subsequent detailed discussions. The author enumerates the sections in order, starting with determination and ending with causality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that it lists five sections.
The passage is an outline, so it may seem dry at first.
2
Identify the five sections
List the five sections: determination, necessity/impossibility/contingency, eternity/temporality, unity/plurality, causality/effect.
3
Consider the logical flow
Think about why the author might have chosen this order. Does it move from more fundamental concepts to relational ones?
This is a preview; deeper explanations come later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : في التعين . الفصل الثالث في لواحق الوجود والماهية وهو مناهج الثاني : في الوجوب والامتناع والإمكان. الثالث : في القدم والحدوث. الرابع: في الوحدة والكثرة . الخامس: في العلية والمعلولية.
The first [section]: on determination. The third chapter on the attachments of existence and essence—and it is [a] method. The second: on necessity, impossibility, and contingency. The third: on eternity and temporality. The fourth: on unity and plurality. The fifth: on causality and effect.
Source pages p. 433

Commentary
This passage is a structural enumeration. It presents the outline of the third chapter (الفصل الثالث) which deals with the 'attachments' (لواحق) of existence and essence. The chapter is said to be 'مناهج' (methods/paths). It then lists five sections: the first on determination (التعين), the second on necessity, impossibility, and contingency (الوجوب والامتناع والإمكان), the third on eternity and temporality (القدم والحدوث), the fourth on unity and plurality (الوحدة والكثرة), and the fifth on causality and effect (العلية والمعلولية). This provides a roadmap for the subsequent discussions in the chapter.

Classifications of the Corollaries of Existence and Quiddity
Sanusi reviews three scholars' classifications of the corollaries of existence and quiddity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how different kalām scholars classified the corollaries (lawāḥiq) of existence and quiddity, and why the author adopts a general inclusive arrangement.

Core Idea
Scholars differ on where to place corollaries, but their approaches reveal deeper methodological commitments.

Before diving into the specific corollaries, Sanusi reviews how previous major scholars organized these concepts. This is not a mere historical footnote; it shows that classifications in kalām are not arbitrary but reflect systematic choices. Understanding these differences helps you appreciate the flexibility and coherence of the author's own approach, which he presents next.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the author's plan to structure the chapter into sections.
The passage begins with 'the third chapter on the corollaries'.
2
Identify the three scholars' classifications
For each scholar (al-Tajrid, al-Mawaqif, al-Saha'if), list how they placed each corollary (necessity, possibility, impossibility, eternity, origination, individuation, unity, multiplicity, cause, effect).
Pay attention to which heading each corollary falls under: existence, quiddity, or separate chapter.
3
Analyze the author's strategy
Why does Sanusi make a general statement that all are corollaries of existence and quiddity? How does this avoid disagreement between the different systems?
He uses an inclusive classification to be valid according to all arrangements.
4
Reflect on methodological significance
What does this tell us about how kalām works? Different scholars can have different systematic arrangements without conflict, as long as the core concepts are addressed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : الفصل الثالث (۱) في لواحق الوجود والماهية، ولنجعله مناهج (۳)، المنهج الأول في التعين (٣) وفيه مباحث) . (۳) جعل صاحب التجريد الوجوب، والإمكان والامتناع، وكذا القدم والحدوث في فصل الوجود، وجعل التعين وكذا الوحدة والكثرة في فصل الماهية وجعل العلة والمعلول، فصلاً على حدة، وصاحب المواقف(٤) جعل التعين في فصل الماهية والوجوب ومقابليه فصلاً على حدة، وكذا الوحدة والكثرة، وكذا العلة والمعلول، وذكر القدم والحدوث في فصل الوجوب ومقابليه ، وصاحب الصحائف (٥) جعل الوجوب ،ومقـابليه والعلة والمعلول من لواحق الموجود والبواقي من لواحق الوجود، فأطلقنا القول بكون الكل من لواحق الوجود) (٦) ، والماهية ليصح على جميع التقارير .
He said: The third chapter on the corollaries of existence and quiddity, and let us make it sections, the first section on individuation and it contains inquiries. The author of al-Tajrid placed necessity, possibility, and impossibility, as well as eternity and origination in the chapter on existence; and placed individuation as well as unity and multiplicity in the chapter on quiddity; and placed cause and effect as a separate chapter. And the author of al-Mawaqif placed individuation in the chapter on quiddity, and necessity and its counterparts in a separate chapter, and likewise unity and multiplicity, and likewise cause and effect; and mentioned eternity and origination in the chapter on necessity and its counterparts. And the author of al-Saha'if placed necessity and its counterparts and cause and effect among the corollaries of the existent, and the rest among the corollaries of existence. So we made a general statement that all are corollaries of existence and quiddity, to be valid according to all arrangements.
Source pages p. 434

Commentary
This passage is a classification and methodological discussion. The author begins by introducing the third chapter on the corollaries (lawāḥiq) of existence (wujūd) and quiddity (māhiyya). He proposes to arrange it into several sections, the first being on individuation (taʿayyun). Then he reviews how different prominent scholars (aṣḥāb) classified these concepts: the author of al-Tajrīd (Naṣīr al-Dīn al-Ṭūsī) placed necessity (wujūb), possibility (imkān), impossibility (imtināʿ), eternity (qidam), and origination (ḥudūth) under existence; individuation (taʿayyun), unity (waḥda), and multiplicity (kathra) under quiddity; and cause and effect (ʿilla wa maʿlūl) as a separate chapter. The author of al-Mawāqif (ʿAḍud al-Dīn al-Ījī) placed individuation under quiddity, necessity and its counterparts (i.e., possibility and impossibility) in a separate chapter, and similarly unity and multiplicity, and cause and effect, while mentioning eternity and origination within the chapter on necessity. The author of al-Ṣaḥāʾif (Mullā ʿAlī al-Qūshjī) placed necessity and its counterparts and cause and effect among the corollaries of the existent (mawjūd), and the rest among the corollaries of existence. The author then explains that he has made a general statement that all these are corollaries of existence and quiddity so that his arrangement is valid according to all these different schemes. This shows methodological flexibility and awareness of different systematic approaches in kalām.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the author is merely listing opinions without purpose.
Correction: He is actually justifying his own inclusive classification by showing the variety of valid approaches.

Key Takeaway
The author adopts a general statement that all corollaries belong to both existence and quiddity, accommodating different scholarly systems.

?

This passage reveals that even foundational concepts like existence and quiddity can be classified in various ways. How does this affect your certainty about theological categories? Are you open to refining your understanding as you encounter more perspectives, or do you feel threatened by diversity of opinion?

The Methodology of Grouping Corollaries
The author reviews three kalam scholars' arrangements of corollaries and justifies his own inclusive approach.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author of Sharh al-Maqasid groups all corollaries under existence and quiddity, and how this accommodates different scholarly approaches.

Core Idea
The author's inclusive arrangement respects all earlier scholars' classifications.

This passage is a methodological reflection. The author presents three different ways that previous kalam authorities organized the corollaries of existence and quiddity. Instead of picking one, he creates a single umbrella chapter that covers all their divisions, showing his work is built on and compatible with the tradition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the names of the scholars and their different placements of topics like determination, necessity, priority, etc.
The translation is literal; the commentary will clarify the details.
2
Identify the three scholars' schemes
The passage mentions three authorities: al-Tajrid, al-Mawaqif, and al-Saha'if. List which corollaries each places under existence, under quiddity, or separately.
Pay attention to 'determination' (التعين) – both al-Tajrid and al-Mawaqif place it under quiddity.
3
Understand the author's own choice
The author says he uses a general heading 'corollaries of existence and quiddity' so his arrangement works for all accounts. Why might that be important for a commentary like Sharh al-Maqasid?
This shows the author's desire for comprehensiveness and respect for tradition.
4
Engage with the guided breakdown
Go through the structured breakdown of the passage. Notice how each scholar's system is described and how the author's solution resolves the differences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثالث في لواحق الوجود والماهية. قال: الفصل الثالث في لواحق الوجود والماهية، ولنجعله مناهج، المنهج الأول في التعين وفيه مباحث. جعل صاحب التجريد الوجوب، والإمكان والامتناع، وكذا القدم والحدوث في فصل الوجود، وجعل التعين وكذا الوحدة والكثرة في فصل الماهية وجعل العلة والمعلول، فصلاً على حدة، وصاحب المواقف جعل التعين في فصل الماهية والوجوب ومقابليه فصلاً على حدة، وكذا الوحدة والكثرة، وكذا العلة والمعلول، وذكر القدم والحدوث في فصل الوجوب ومقابليه، وصاحب الصحائف جعل الوجوب، ومقابليه والعلة والمعلول من لواحق الموجود والبواقي من لواحق الوجود، فأطلقنا القول بكون الكل من لواحق الوجود والماهية ليصح على جميع التقارير.
The third chapter on the corollaries of existence and quiddity. He said: The third chapter on the corollaries of existence and quiddity, and let us make it [into] sections, the first section on determination, and in it are investigations. The author of al-Tajrid made necessity, contingency, impossibility, as well as priority and newness, in the chapter on existence, and he made determination as well as unity and multiplicity in the chapter on quiddity, and he made cause and effect as a separate chapter. The author of al-Mawaqif made determination in the chapter on quiddity, and necessity and its opposites as a separate chapter, and likewise unity and multiplicity, and likewise cause and effect, and he mentioned priority and newness in the chapter on necessity and its opposites. The author of al-Saha'if made necessity and its opposites and cause and effect from the corollaries of the existent, and the rest from the corollaries of existence. So we have stated generally that all are from the corollaries of existence and quiddity, so that it is valid according to all accounts.
Source pages p. 435

Commentary
This passage is a methodological discussion about the arrangement of the corollaries (لواحق) of existence (الوجود) and quiddity (الماهية) in kalam works. The author begins by stating the title of the third chapter, which covers these corollaries, and then explains why he organizes them as he does. He notes that different scholars have placed these topics in different chapters. Specifically, the author of al-Tajrid (likely Nasir al-Din al-Tusi) placed necessity, contingency, impossibility, priority, and newness under the chapter on existence, but placed determination, unity, and multiplicity under quiddity, and made cause and effect a separate chapter. The author of al-Mawaqif (al-Iji) placed determination under quiddity, made necessity and its opposites a separate chapter, and also separated unity/multiplicity and cause/effect, but he placed priority and newness under necessity and its opposites. The author of al-Saha'if (al-Samarqandi) considered necessity, its opposites, and cause/effect as corollaries of the existent (الموجود), and the rest as corollaries of existence. Given this diversity, the author of this work (Sharh al-Maqasid) has chosen to treat all these topics under the combined heading of 'corollaries of existence and quiddity' so that his arrangement is compatible with all the different approaches. This demonstrates awareness of the scholarly tradition and a desire for comprehensiveness.

العلة والمعلول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author is merely listing opinions without a clear position of his own.
Correction: The author explicitly states his own inclusive approach: using the general heading 'corollaries of existence and quiddity' to cover all corollaries, so that his arrangement is valid according to all accounts.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The author's decision to group all corollaries under existence and quiddity is a deliberate method to respect and encompass the diverse arrangements of earlier kalam authorities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter differing classifications or opinions among scholars, does your initial reaction tend to be frustration, curiosity, or dismissal? How might adopting the author's inclusive approach change the way you engage with diverse views in your own learning?

What is one practical step you can take this week to adopt a more inclusive attitude for the sake of Allah?
?

Where in your faith practice are you either uncritically following tradition or dismissively ignoring it? How can you strike the balance the author demonstrates?
